<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For representative democracy that operates under the principles of classical liberalism, see <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">Liberal democracy</a>. <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer thumbborder" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Basic_forms_of_government" title="Template:Basic forms of government"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Basic_forms_of_government" title="Template talk:Basic forms of government"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Basic_forms_of_government&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Democracy</b> (From <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">δημοκρατία</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Ancient_Greek" title="Romanization of Ancient Greek">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Ancient Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">dēmokratía</i></span>, <i>dēmos</i> 'people' and <i>kratos</i> 'rule'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-1">[1]</a></sup>) is a form of <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a> in which <a href="/wiki/People" title="People">the people</a> have the <a href="/wiki/Authority" title="Authority">authority</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deliberate" title="Deliberate">deliberate</a> and decide legislation ("<a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a>"), or to choose governing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Officials" title="Officials">officials</a> to do so ("<a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democracy</a>"). Who is considered part of "the people" and how authority is shared among or delegated by the people has changed over time and at different rates in different countries, but over time more and more of a democratic country's inhabitants have generally been included. Cornerstones of democracy include <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_assembly" title="Freedom of assembly">freedom of assembly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_association" title="Freedom of association">association</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Property_rights" title="Property rights">property rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">freedom of religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">speech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_exclusion#Social_inclusion" title="Social exclusion">inclusiveness</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_equality" title="Political equality">equality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Citizenship" title="Citizenship">citizenship</a>, <a href="/wiki/Consent_of_the_governed" title="Consent of the governed">consent of the governed</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voting_rights" title="Voting rights">voting rights</a>, freedom from unwarranted governmental <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/deprivation" title="wikt:deprivation">deprivation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_life" title="Right to life">right to life</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">liberty</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Minority_rights" title="Minority rights">minority rights</a>.
</p><p>The notion of democracy has evolved over time considerably. The original form of democracy was a direct democracy. The most common form of democracy today is a <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democracy</a>, where the people elect government officials to govern on their behalf such as in a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presidential_democracy" title="Presidential democracy">presidential democracy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tangian2020Book_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tangian2020Book-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prevalent day-to-day decision making of democracies is the <a href="/wiki/Majority_rule" title="Majority rule">majority rule</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webster_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-webster-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> though other decision making approaches like <a href="/wiki/Supermajority" title="Supermajority">supermajority</a> and <a href="/wiki/Consensus_democracy" title="Consensus democracy">consensus</a> have also been integral to democracies. They serve the crucial purpose of inclusiveness and broader legitimacy on sensitive issues—counterbalancing <a href="/wiki/Majoritarianism" title="Majoritarianism">majoritarianism</a>—and therefore mostly take precedence on a constitutional level. In the common variant of <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a>, the powers of the majority are exercised within the framework of a representative democracy, but the <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitution</a> limits the majority and protects the minority—usually through the enjoyment by all of certain individual rights, e.g. freedom of speech or freedom of association.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brittanica_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brittanica-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term appeared in the 5th century BC in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_city-state" title="Greek city-state">Greek city-states</a>, notably <a href="/wiki/Classical_Athens" title="Classical Athens">Classical Athens</a>, to mean "rule of the people", in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἀριστοκρατία</span></span>, <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">aristokratía</i></span></i>), meaning "rule of an elite".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_democracy" title="Western democracy">Western democracy</a>, as distinct from that which existed in antiquity, is generally considered to have originated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City-states" title="City-states">city-states</a> such as those in Classical Athens and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>, where various schemes and degrees of enfranchisement of the free male population were observed before the form disappeared in <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">the West</a> at the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Late_antiquity" title="Late antiquity">late antiquity</a>. In virtually all democratic governments throughout ancient and modern history, democratic citizenship was initially restricted to an elite class, which was later extended to all adult citizens. In most modern democracies, this was achieved through the <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">suffrage</a> movements of the 19th and 20th centuries.
</p><p>Democracy contrasts with forms of government where power is either held by an individual, as in <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocratic</a> systems like <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a>, or where power is held by a small number of individuals, as in an <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a>—oppositions inherited from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a> defined democracy in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship" title="Dictatorship">dictatorship</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyranny</a>, focusing on opportunities for the people to control their leaders and to oust them without the need for a <a href="/wiki/Revolution" title="Revolution">revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> World public opinion strongly favors democratic systems of government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-4"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Democracy_Index_2020.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="2754" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Democracy_Index_2020.svg/220px-Democracy_Index_2020.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Democracy_Index_2020.svg/330px-Democracy_Index_2020.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Democracy_Index_2020.svg/440px-Democracy_Index_2020.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Democracy_Index_2020.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><table style="width:100%; vertical-align:top; border:1px solid #ABD5F5; background-color:#F1F5FC; margin-top:2px;"> <tbody><tr> <td> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#006837; color:white;"> </span> Most democratic (closest to 10) </div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#a50026; color:white;"> </span> Least democratic (closest to 0)</div> </td></tr></tbody></table>Democracy's <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> status in the world as of 2020, according to <a href="/wiki/Democracy_Index" title="Democracy Index">Democracy Index</a> by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Democracy_claims.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Democracy_claims.svg/220px-Democracy_claims.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Democracy_claims.svg/330px-Democracy_claims.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Democracy_claims.svg/440px-Democracy_claims.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Democracy_claims.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Democracy's <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> status in the world as of 2020; only <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">UAE</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican</a> do not claim to be a democracy.</div></div></div>
<p>Although democracy is generally understood to be defined by voting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brittanica_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brittanica-6">[6]</a></sup> no consensus exists on a precise definition of  democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist2007_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-economist2007-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a> says that the "classical" view of democracy is simply,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> "in brief, the theory that democracy is the rule of the people, and that the people have a right to rule."  <a href="/wiki/Kofi_Annan" title="Kofi Annan">Kofi Annan</a> states that "there are as many different forms of democracy as there are democratic nations in the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> One study identified 2,234 adjectives used to describe democracy in the English language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Democratic principles are reflected in all eligible citizens being <a href="/wiki/Equality_before_the_law" title="Equality before the law">equal before the law</a> and having equal access to legislative processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> For example, in a <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democracy</a>, every vote has equal weight, no unreasonable restrictions can apply to anyone seeking to become a representative,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (January 2015)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> and the freedom of its eligible citizens is secured by legitimised rights and liberties which are typically protected by a <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Other uses of "democracy" include that of <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a>, in which issues are directly voted on by the constituents.
</p><p>One theory holds that democracy requires three fundamental principles: upward control (sovereignty residing at the lowest levels of authority), political equality, and social norms by which individuals and institutions only consider acceptable acts that reflect the first two principles of upward control and political equality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_equality" title="Legal equality">Legal equality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Political_freedom" title="Political freedom">political freedom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> are often identified as foundational characteristics for a well-functioning democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist2007_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-economist2007-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "democracy" is sometimes used as shorthand for <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a>, which is a variant of representative democracy that may include elements such as <a href="/wiki/Pluralism_(political_philosophy)" title="Pluralism (political philosophy)">political pluralism</a>; equality before the law; the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_petition" title="Right to petition">right to petition</a> elected officials for redress of grievances; <a href="/wiki/Due_process" title="Due process">due process</a>; <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberties</a>; <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>; and elements of <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civil society</a> outside the government.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Roger_Scruton" title="Roger Scruton">Roger Scruton</a> argued that democracy alone cannot provide personal and political freedom unless the institutions of <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civil society</a> are also present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some countries, notably in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> which originated the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_system" title="Westminster system">Westminster system</a>, the dominant principle is that of <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_sovereignty" title="Parliamentary sovereignty">parliamentary sovereignty</a>, while maintaining <a href="/wiki/Judicial_independence" title="Judicial independence">judicial independence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kopstein2014_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kopstein2014-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, parliamentary sovereignty is subject to the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_India" title="Constitution of India">Constitution of India</a> which includes <a href="/wiki/Judicial_review" title="Judicial review">judicial review</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Though the term "democracy" is typically used in the context of a <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">political state</a>, the principles also are applicable to private <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation" title="Organisation">organisations</a>.
</p><p>There are many decision-making methods used in democracies, but <a href="/wiki/Majority_rule" title="Majority rule">majority rule</a> is the dominant form. Without compensation, like legal protections of individual or group rights, <a href="/wiki/Minority_group" title="Minority group">political minorities</a> can be oppressed by the "<a href="/wiki/Tyranny_of_the_majority" title="Tyranny of the majority">tyranny of the majority</a>". Majority rule is a competitive approach, opposed to <a href="/wiki/Consensus_democracy" title="Consensus democracy">consensus democracy</a>, creating the need that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elections" title="Elections">elections</a>, and generally <a href="/wiki/Deliberation" title="Deliberation">deliberation</a>, are substantively and procedurally "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fair" title="wikt:fair">fair</a>," i.e. <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/just" title="wikt:just">just</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/equitable" title="wikt:equitable">equitable</a>. In some countries, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_(political)" title="Freedom (political)">freedom of political expression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press" title="Freedom of the press">freedom of the press</a>, and <a href="/wiki/E-democracy" title="E-democracy">internet democracy</a> are considered important to ensure that voters are well informed, enabling them to vote according to their own interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has also been suggested that a basic feature of democracy is the capacity of all voters to participate freely and fully in the life of their society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> With its emphasis on notions of <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> and the collective will of all the voters, democracy can also be characterised as a form of political <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collectivism_and_individualism" title="Collectivism and individualism">collectivism</a> because it is defined as a form of government in which all eligible citizens have an equal say in lawmaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">Republics</a>, though often associated with democracy because of the shared principle of rule by <a href="/wiki/Consent_of_the_governed" title="Consent of the governed">consent of the governed</a>, are not necessarily democracies, as <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a> does not specify <i>how</i> the people are to rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-29">[29]</a></sup>
Classically the term "<a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>"  encompassed both democracies and <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In a modern sense the republican form of government is a form of government without <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarch</a>. Because of this democracies can be republics or <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchies</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_democracy" title="History of democracy">History of democracy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Discurso_funebre_pericles.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="719" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Discurso_funebre_pericles.PNG/220px-Discurso_funebre_pericles.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Discurso_funebre_pericles.PNG/330px-Discurso_funebre_pericles.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Discurso_funebre_pericles.PNG/440px-Discurso_funebre_pericles.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Discurso_funebre_pericles.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nineteenth-century painting by <a href="/wiki/Philipp_Foltz" title="Philipp Foltz">Philipp Foltz</a> depicting the Athenian politician <a href="/wiki/Pericles" title="Pericles">Pericles</a> delivering his famous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pericles%27_funeral_oration" title="Pericles' funeral oration">funeral oration</a> in front of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecclesia_(ancient_Athens)" title="Ecclesia (ancient Athens)">Assembly</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></div></div></div><p>Historically, democracies and republics have been rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-33">[33]</a></sup> Republican theorists linked democracy to small size: as political units grew in size, the likelihood increased that the government would turn despotic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> At the same time, small political units were vulnerable to conquest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a> wrote, "If a republic be small, it is destroyed by a foreign force; if it be large, it is ruined by an internal imperfection."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> According to Johns Hopkins University political scientist <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Deudney" title="Daniel Deudney">Daniel Deudney</a>, the creation of the United States, with its large size and its system of checks and balances, was a solution to the dual problems of size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears (August 2022)">pages needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Retrospectively different polities, outside of declared democracies, have been described as proto-democratic.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Athenian_democracy" title="Athenian democracy">Athenian democracy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democratic_elements_of_Roman_Republic" title="Democratic elements of Roman Republic">Democratic elements of Roman Republic</a></div>
<p>The term <i>democracy</i> first appeared in ancient Greek political and philosophical thought in the city-state of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> during <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaaflaubOberWallace2007[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_July_2014]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(July_2014)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_37-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaaflaubOberWallace2007[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_July_2014]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(July_2014)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-37'>[37]</a></sup> The word comes from <i>dêmos</i> '(common) people' and <i>krátos</i> 'force/might'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Under <a href="/wiki/Cleisthenes" title="Cleisthenes">Cleisthenes</a>,  what is generally held as the first example of a type of democracy in 508–507 BC was established in Athens. Cleisthenes is referred to as "the father of <a href="/wiki/Athenian_democracy" title="Athenian democracy">Athenian democracy</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The first attested use of the word democracy is found in prose works of the 430s BC, such as <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">Histories</a></i>, but its usage was older by several decades, as two Athenians born in the 470s were named Democrates, a new political name—likely in support of democracy—given at a time of debates over constitutional issues in Athens. <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">Aeschylus</a> also strongly alludes to the word in his play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Suppliants_(Aeschylus)" title="The Suppliants (Aeschylus)">The Suppliants</a></i>, staged in c.463 BC, where he mentions "the demos’s ruling hand" [<i>demou kratousa cheir</i>]. Before that time, the word used to define the new political system of Cleisthenes was probably <a href="/wiki/Isonomia" title="Isonomia">isonomia</a>, meaning political equality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Athenian democracy took the form of a direct democracy, and it had two distinguishing features: the <a href="/wiki/Sortition" title="Sortition">random selection</a> of ordinary citizens to fill the few existing government administrative and judicial offices,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and a legislative assembly consisting of all Athenian citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> All eligible citizens were allowed to speak and vote in the assembly, which set the laws of the city state. However, Athenian citizenship excluded women, slaves, foreigners (μέτοικοι / <i>métoikoi</i>), and youths below the age of military service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i>[[Athenian democracy#{{{section}}}|<span title="This text contradicts material at the article 'Athenian democracy'. (September 2014)">contradictory</span>]]</i>]</sup> Effectively, only 1 in 4 residents in Athens qualified as citizens. Owning land was not a requirement for citizenship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The exclusion of large parts of the population from the citizen body is closely related to the ancient understanding of citizenship. In most of antiquity the benefit of citizenship was tied to the obligation to fight war campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Athenian democracy was not only <i>direct</i> in the sense that decisions were made by the assembled people, but also the <i>most direct</i> in the sense that the people through the assembly, <a href="/wiki/Boule_(ancient_Greece)" title="Boule (ancient Greece)">boule</a> and courts of law controlled the entire political process and a large proportion of citizens were involved constantly in the public business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaaflaubOberWallace20075_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaaflaubOberWallace20075-47">[47]</a></sup> Even though the rights of the individual were not secured by the Athenian constitution in the modern sense (the ancient Greeks had no word for "rights"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOberHedrick1996107_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOberHedrick1996107-48">[48]</a></sup>), those who were citizens of Athens enjoyed their liberties not in opposition to the government but by living in a city that was not subject to another power and by not being subjects themselves to the rule of another person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarkeFoweraker2001194–201_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarkeFoweraker2001194–201-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Range_voting" title="Range voting">Range voting</a> appeared in <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a> as early as 700 BC. The <a href="/wiki/Ecclesia_(Sparta)" title="Ecclesia (Sparta)">Spartan ecclesia</a> was an assembly of the people, held once a month, in which every male citizen of at least 20 years of age could participate. In the assembly, Spartans elected leaders and cast votes by range voting and shouting (the vote is then decided on how loudly the crowd shouts). <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> called this "childish", as compared with the stone voting ballots used by the Athenian citizenry. Sparta adopted it because of its simplicity, and to prevent any biased voting, buying, or cheating that was predominant in the early democratic elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even though the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> contributed significantly to many aspects of democracy, only a minority of Romans were citizens with votes in elections for representatives. The votes of the powerful were given more weight through a system of <a href="/wiki/Weighted_voting" title="Weighted voting">weighted voting</a>, so most high officials, including members of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Senate</a>, came from a few wealthy and noble families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In addition, the <a href="/wiki/Overthrow_of_the_Roman_monarchy" title="Overthrow of the Roman monarchy">overthrow of the Roman Kingdom</a> was the first case in the Western world of a polity being formed with the explicit purpose of being a <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>, although it didn't have much of a democracy. The Roman model of governance inspired many political thinkers over the centuries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and today's modern representative democracies imitate more the Roman than the Greek models because it was a state in which supreme power was held by the people and their elected representatives, and which had an elected or nominated leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Vaishali_(ancient_city)" title="Vaishali (ancient city)">Vaishali</a>, capital city of the <a href="/wiki/Vajjika_League" title="Vajjika League">Vajjika League</a> (Vrijji <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahajanapada" title="Mahajanapada">mahajanapada</a>) of, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> was also considered one of the first examples of a <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a> around the 6th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other cultures, such as the <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">Iroquois</a> Nation in the Americas also developed a form of democratic society between 1450 and 1660 (and possibly in 1142<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMannFields1997_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMannFields1997-58">[58]</a></sup>), well before contact with the Europeans. This democracy continues to the present day and is the world's oldest standing representative democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> This indicates that forms of democracy may have been invented in other societies around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While most regions in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> were ruled by <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">clergy</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feudal_lords" title="Feudal lords">feudal lords</a>, there existed various systems involving elections or assemblies, although often only involving a small part of the population. In <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, bodies known as <a href="/wiki/Thing_(assembly)" title="Thing (assembly)">things</a> consisted of freemen presided by a <a href="/wiki/Lawspeaker" title="Lawspeaker">lawspeaker</a>. These deliberative bodies were responsible for settling political questions, and variants included the <a href="/wiki/Althing" title="Althing">Althing</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/L%C3%B8gting" title="Løgting">Løgting</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faeroe_Islands" title="Faeroe Islands">Faeroe Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Veche" title="Veche">veche</a>, found in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, was a similar body to the Scandinavian thing. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">pope</a> has been elected by a <a href="/wiki/Papal_conclave" title="Papal conclave">papal conclave</a> composed of cardinals since 1059. The first documented parliamentary body in Europe was the <a href="/wiki/Cortes_of_Le%C3%B3n_of_1188" title="Cortes of León of 1188">Cortes of León</a>. Established by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfonso_IX" title="Alfonso IX">Alfonso IX</a> in 1188, the Cortes had authority over setting taxation, foreign affairs and legislating, though the exact nature of its role remains disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ragusa" title="Republic of Ragusa">Republic of Ragusa</a>, established in 1358 and centered around the city of <a href="/wiki/Dubrovnik" title="Dubrovnik">Dubrovnik</a>, provided representation and voting rights to its male aristocracy only. Various Italian city-states and polities had republic forms of government. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Florence" title="Republic of Florence">Republic of Florence</a>, established in 1115, was led by the <a href="/wiki/Signoria_of_Florence" title="Signoria of Florence">Signoria</a> whose members were chosen by <a href="/wiki/Sortition" title="Sortition">sortition</a>. In 10th–15th century <a href="/wiki/Frisian_freedom" title="Frisian freedom">Frisia</a>, a distinctly non-feudal society, the right to vote on local matters and on county officials was based on land size. The <a href="/wiki/Kouroukan_Fouga" title="Kouroukan Fouga">Kouroukan Fouga</a> divided the <a href="/wiki/Mali_Empire" title="Mali Empire">Mali Empire</a> into ruling clans (lineages) that were represented at a great assembly called the <i>Gbara</i>. However, the charter made Mali more similar to a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> than a <a href="/wiki/Democratic_republic" title="Democratic republic">democratic republic</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magna_Carta_(British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4800" data-file-width="7200" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Magna_Carta_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106%29.jpg/290px-Magna_Carta_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Magna_Carta_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106%29.jpg/435px-Magna_Carta_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Magna_Carta_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106%29.jpg/580px-Magna_Carta_%28British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106%29.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magna_Carta_(British_Library_Cotton_MS_Augustus_II.106).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>, 1215, England</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a> had its roots in the restrictions on the power of kings written into <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a> (1215), which explicitly protected certain rights of the King's subjects and implicitly supported what became the English writ of <a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a>, safeguarding individual freedom against unlawful imprisonment with right to appeal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The first representative national assembly in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a> was <a href="/wiki/Simon_de_Montfort%27s_Parliament" title="Simon de Montfort's Parliament">Simon de Montfort's Parliament</a> in 1265.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The emergence of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England#Emergence_as_an_institution" title="Parliament of England">petitioning</a> is some of the earliest evidence of parliament being used as a forum to address the general grievances of ordinary people. However, the power to call parliament remained at the pleasure of the monarch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies have linked the emergence of parliamentary institutions in Europe during the medieval period to urban agglomeration and the creation of new classes, such as artisans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> as well as the presence of nobility and religious elites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Scholars have also linked the emergence of representative government to Europe's relative political fragmentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Political scientist <a href="/wiki/David_Stasavage" title="David Stasavage">David Stasavage</a> links the fragmentation of Europe, and its subsequent democratization, to the manner in which the Roman Empire collapsed: Roman territory was conquered by small fragmented groups of Germanic tribes, thus leading to the creation of small political units where rulers were relatively weak and needed the consent of the governed to ward off foreign threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, noble democracy was characterized by an increase in the activity of the middle <a href="/wiki/Szlachta" title="Szlachta">nobility</a>, which wanted to increase their share in exercising power at the expense of the magnates. Magnates dominated the most important offices in the state (secular and ecclesiastical) and sat on the royal council, later the senate. The growing importance of the middle nobility had an impact on the establishment of the institution of the land <i><a href="/wiki/Sejm" title="Sejm">sejmik</a></i> (local assembly), which subsequently obtained more rights. During the fifteenth and first half of the sixteenth century, sejmiks received more and more powers and became the most important institutions of local power. In 1454, <a href="/wiki/Casimir_IV_Jagiellon" title="Casimir IV Jagiellon">Casimir IV Jagiellon</a> granted the sejmiks the right to decide on taxes and to convene a mass mobilization in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nieszawa_Statutes" title="Nieszawa Statutes">Nieszawa Statutes</a>. He also pledged not to create new laws without their consent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Modern era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_period">Early modern period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early modern period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Locke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1138" data-file-width="984" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/John_Locke.jpg/200px-John_Locke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/John_Locke.jpg/300px-John_Locke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/John_Locke.jpg/400px-John_Locke.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Locke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> expanded on <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_contract_theory" title="Social contract theory">social contract theory</a> and developed the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_rights" title="Natural rights">natural rights</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_property" title="Right to property">right to private property</a> and the principle of <a href="/wiki/Consent_of_the_governed" title="Consent of the governed">consent of the governed</a>. His ideas form the ideological basis of <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracies</a> today.</div></div></div>
<p>In 17th century England, there was <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta#17th–18th_centuries" title="Magna Carta">renewed interest in Magna Carta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The Parliament of England passed the <a href="/wiki/Petition_of_Right" title="Petition of Right">Petition of Right</a> in 1628 which established certain liberties for subjects. The <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> (1642–1651) was fought between the King and an oligarchic but elected Parliament,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> during which the idea of a political party took form with groups debating rights to political representation during the <a href="/wiki/Putney_Debates" title="Putney Debates">Putney Debates</a> of 1647.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Subsequently, <a href="/wiki/The_Protectorate" title="The Protectorate">the Protectorate</a> (1653–59) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Restoration" title="English Restoration">English Restoration</a> (1660) restored more autocratic rule, although Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Habeas_Corpus_Act_1679" title="Habeas Corpus Act 1679">Habeas Corpus Act</a> in 1679 which strengthened the convention that forbade detention lacking sufficient cause or evidence. After the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> of 1688, the <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights</a> was enacted in 1689 which codified certain rights and liberties and is still in effect. The Bill set out the requirement for regular elections, rules for freedom of speech in Parliament and limited the power of the monarch, ensuring that, unlike much of Europe at the time, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_absolutism" title="Royal absolutism">royal absolutism</a> would not prevail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-refNARoP_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-refNARoP-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Economic historians <a href="/wiki/Douglass_North" title="Douglass North">Douglass North</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barry_R._Weingast" title="Barry R. Weingast">Barry Weingast</a> have characterized the institutions implemented in the Glorious Revolution as a resounding success in terms of restraining the government and ensuring protection for property rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Renewed interest in the Magna Carta, the English Civil War, and the Glorious Revolution in the 17th century prompted the growth of <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political philosophy</a> on the British Isles. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> was the first philosopher to articulate a detailed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_contract_theory" title="Social contract theory">social contract theory</a>. Writing in <i><a href="/wiki/Leviathan_(Hobbes_book)" title="Leviathan (Hobbes book)">Leviathan</a></i> (1651), Hobbes theorized that individuals living in the <a href="/wiki/State_of_nature" title="State of nature">state of nature</a> led lives that were "solitary, poor, nasty, brutish and short" and constantly waged a <a href="/wiki/Bellum_omnium_contra_omnes" title="Bellum omnium contra omnes">war of all against all</a>. In order to prevent the occurrence of an anarchic state of nature, Hobbes reasoned that individuals ceded their rights to a strong, authoritarian government. Later, philosopher and physician <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> would posit a different interpretation of social contract theory. Writing in his <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises of Government</a></i> (1689), Locke posited that all individuals possessed the inalienable rights to life, liberty and estate (property).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Locke-Laslett1988_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Locke-Laslett1988-81">[81]</a></sup> According to Locke, individuals would voluntarily come together to form a state for the purposes of defending their rights. Particularly important for Locke were property rights, whose protection Locke deemed to be a government's primary purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Furthermore, Locke asserted that governments were <a href="/wiki/Legitimacy_(political)" title="Legitimacy (political)">legitimate</a> only if they held the <a href="/wiki/Consent_of_the_governed" title="Consent of the governed">consent of the governed</a>. For Locke, citizens had the <a href="/wiki/Right_of_revolution" title="Right of revolution">right to revolt</a> against a government that acted against their interest or became tyrannical. Although they were not widely read during his lifetime, Locke's works are considered the founding documents of <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> thought and profoundly influenced the leaders of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> and later the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> His liberal democratic framework of governance remains the preeminent form of democracy in the world.
</p><p>In the Cossack republics of Ukraine in the 16th and 17th centuries, the <a href="/wiki/Cossack_Hetmanate" title="Cossack Hetmanate">Cossack Hetmanate</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zaporizhian_Sich" title="Zaporizhian Sich">Zaporizhian Sich</a>, the holder of the highest post of <a href="/wiki/Hetmans_of_Ukrainian_Cossacks" title="Hetmans of Ukrainian Cossacks">Hetman</a> was elected by the representatives from the country's districts.
</p><p>In North America, representative government began in <a href="/wiki/Jamestown,_Virginia" title="Jamestown, Virginia">Jamestown, Virginia</a>, with the election of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Burgesses" title="House of Burgesses">House of Burgesses</a> (forerunner of the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_General_Assembly" title="Virginia General Assembly">Virginia General Assembly</a>) in 1619. English Puritans who migrated from 1620 established colonies in New England whose local governance was democratic;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> although these local assemblies had some small amounts of devolved power, the ultimate authority was held by the Crown and the English Parliament. The <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pilgrim_Fathers" title="Pilgrim Fathers">Pilgrim Fathers</a>), <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a> who founded these colonies applied the democratic organisation of their congregations also to the administration of their communities in worldly matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="18th_and_19th_centuries">18th and 19th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 18th and 19th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Austria_Parlament_Athena_bw.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1820" data-file-width="1243" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Austria_Parlament_Athena_bw.jpg/170px-Austria_Parlament_Athena_bw.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Austria_Parlament_Athena_bw.jpg/255px-Austria_Parlament_Athena_bw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Austria_Parlament_Athena_bw.jpg/340px-Austria_Parlament_Athena_bw.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Austria_Parlament_Athena_bw.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>, the patron goddess of <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, in front of the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Parliament_Building" title="Austrian Parliament Building">Austrian Parliament Building</a>. Athena has been used as an international symbol of freedom and democracy since at least the late eighteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/First_Parliament_of_Great_Britain" title="First Parliament of Great Britain">first Parliament of Great Britain</a> was established in 1707, after the merger of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Kingdom of Scotland</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union</a>. Although the monarch increasingly became a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">figurehead</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Parliament was only elected by male property owners, which amounted to 3% of the population in 1780.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The first known British person of <a href="/wiki/African_diaspora" title="African diaspora">African</a> heritage to vote in a general election, <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_Sancho" title="Ignatius Sancho">Ignatius Sancho</a>, voted in 1774 and 1780.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Liberty" title="Age of Liberty">Age of Liberty</a> in Sweden (1718–1772), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a> were expanded and power shifted from the monarch to parliament. The taxed peasantry was represented in parliament, although with little influence, but commoners without taxed property had no suffrage.
</p><p>The creation of the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Corsican_Republic" title="Corsican Republic">Corsican Republic</a> in 1755 was an early attempt to adopt a democratic <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitution</a> (all men and women above age of 25 could vote).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Corsican_Constitution" title="Corsican Constitution">Corsican Constitution</a> was the first based on <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> principles and included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Female_suffrage" title="Female suffrage">female suffrage</a>, something that was not included in most other democracies until the 20th century.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Colonial_history_of_the_United_States" title="Colonial history of the United States">American colonial period before 1776</a>, and for some time after, often only adult white male property owners could vote; enslaved Africans, most free black people and most women were not extended the franchise. This changed state by state, beginning with the republican State of New Connecticut, soon after called <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>, which, on declaring independence of Great Britain in 1777, adopted a constitution modelled on Pennsylvania's with citizenship and democratic suffrage for males with or without property, and went on to abolish slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> led to the adoption of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a> in 1787, the oldest surviving, still active, governmental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codified_constitution" title="Codified constitution">codified constitution</a>. The Constitution provided for an elected government and protected civil rights and liberties for some, but did not end <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slavery</a> nor extend <a href="/wiki/Voting_rights_in_the_United_States" title="Voting rights in the United States">voting rights in the United States</a>, instead leaving the issue of suffrage to the individual states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Generally, states limited suffrage to white male property owners and taxpayers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> At the time of the first <a href="/wiki/1788%E2%80%9389_United_States_presidential_election" title="1788–89 United States presidential election">Presidential election in 1789</a>, about 6% of the population was eligible to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Naturalization_Act_of_1790" title="Naturalization Act of 1790">Naturalization Act of 1790</a> limited U.S. citizenship to whites only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schultz_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schultz-98">[98]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bill_of_Rights" title="United States Bill of Rights">Bill of Rights</a> in 1791 set limits on government power to protect personal freedoms but had little impact on judgements by the courts for the first 130 years after ratification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1789, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolutionary_France" title="Revolutionary France">Revolutionary France</a> adopted the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen</a> and, although short-lived, the <a href="/wiki/National_Convention" title="National Convention">National Convention</a> was elected by all men in 1792.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_3_May_1791" title="Constitution of 3 May 1791">Polish-Lithuanian Constitution</a> of 3 May 1791 sought to implement a more effective <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>, introduced political equality between townspeople and nobility, and placed the peasants under the protection of the government, mitigating the worst abuses of <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a>. In force for less than 19 months, it was declared null and void by the <a href="/wiki/Grodno_Sejm" title="Grodno Sejm">Grodno Sejm</a> that met in 1793.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies1992_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies1992-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ligeza2017_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ligeza2017-102">[102]</a></sup> Nonetheless, the 1791 Constitution helped keep alive Polish aspirations for the eventual restoration of the country's sovereignty over a century later.
</p><p>However, in the early 19th century, little of democracy—as theory, practice, or even as word—remained in the North Atlantic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> During this period, <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> remained a social and economic institution in places around the world. This was particularly the case in the United States, where eight serving presidents had owned slaves, and the last fifteen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_states" title="Slave states">slave states</a> kept slavery legal in the American South until the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Advocating the movement of black people from the US to locations where they would enjoy greater freedom and equality, in the 1820s the abolitionist members of the <a href="/wiki/American_Colonization_Society" title="American Colonization Society">ACS</a> established the settlement of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The United Kingdom's <a href="/wiki/Slave_Trade_Act_1807" title="Slave Trade Act 1807">Slave Trade Act 1807</a> banned the trade across the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, which was <a href="/wiki/Blockade_of_Africa" title="Blockade of Africa">enforced internationally</a> by the Royal Navy under treaties Britain negotiated with other states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> In 1833, the UK passed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavery_Abolition_Act" title="Slavery Abolition Act">Slavery Abolition Act</a> which took effect across the British Empire, although slavery was legally allowed to continue in areas controlled by the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceylon" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">Saint Helena</a> for an <a href="/wiki/Indian_Slavery_Act,_1843" title="Indian Slavery Act, 1843">additional ten years</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2568" data-file-width="3476" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg/220px-Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg/330px-Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg/440px-Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Suffrage_universel_1848.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The establishment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_male_suffrage" title="Universal male suffrage">universal male suffrage</a> in France in 1848 was an important milestone in the history of democracy.</div></div></div>
<p>In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/1828_United_States_presidential_election" title="1828 United States presidential election">1828 presidential election</a> was the first in which non-property-holding white males could vote in the vast majority of states. Voter turnout soared during the 1830s, reaching about 80% of the adult white male population in the <a href="/wiki/1840_United_States_presidential_election" title="1840 United States presidential election">1840 presidential election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> North Carolina was the last state to abolish property qualification in 1856 resulting in a close approximation to universal white male suffrage (however tax-paying requirements remained in five states in 1860 and survived in two states until the 20th century).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engerman2005_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Engerman2005-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scher2015_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scher2015-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHLTS2009_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHLTS2009-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[nb 1]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1860_United_States_Census" title="1860 United States Census">1860 United States Census</a>, the slave population had grown to four million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction era of the United States">Reconstruction</a> after the Civil War, three constitutional amendments were passed: the <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">13th Amendment</a> (1865) that ended slavery; the <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">14th Amendment</a> (1869) that gave black people citizenship, and the <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">15th Amendment</a> (1870) that gave black males a nominal right to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> Full enfranchisement of citizens was not secured until after the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a> gained passage by the US Congress of the <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The voting franchise in the United Kingdom was expanded and made more uniform in a series of reforms that began with the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a> and continued into the 20th century, notably with the <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1918" title="Representation of the People Act 1918">Representation of the People Act 1918</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equal_Franchise_Act_1928" title="Equal Franchise Act 1928">Equal Franchise Act 1928</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_male_suffrage" title="Universal male suffrage">Universal male suffrage</a> was established in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> in March 1848 in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution_of_1848" title="French Revolution of 1848">French Revolution of 1848</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> In 1848, several <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">revolutions broke out in Europe</a> as rulers were confronted with popular demands for liberal constitutions and more democratic government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1876 the Ottoman Empire transitioned from an absolute monarchy to a constitutional one, and held two elections the next year to elect members to her newly formed parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> Provisional Electoral Regulations were issued, stating that the elected members of the Provincial Administrative Councils would elect members to the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Parliament of the Ottoman Empire">Parliament</a>. Later that year, a new constitution was promulgated, which provided for a <a href="/wiki/Bicameralism" title="Bicameralism">bicameral</a> Parliament with a <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Senate of the Ottoman Empire">Senate</a> appointed by <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Hamid_II" title="Abdul Hamid II">the Sultan</a> and a popularly elected <a href="/wiki/Chamber_of_Deputies_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Chamber of Deputies (Ottoman Empire)">Chamber of Deputies</a>. Only men above the age of 30 who were competent in <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> and had full civil rights were allowed to stand for election. Reasons for disqualification included holding dual citizenship, being employed by a foreign government, being bankrupt, employed as a servant, or having "notoriety for ill deeds". Full universal suffrage was achieved in 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1893 the self-governing colony <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> became the first country in the world (except for the short-lived 18th-century Corsican Republic) to establish active <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a> by recognizing women as having the right to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_and_21st_centuries">20th and 21st centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 20th and 21st centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Number_of_nations_1800-2003_scoring_8_or_higher_on_Polity_IV_scale.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="561" data-file-width="949" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Number_of_nations_1800-2003_scoring_8_or_higher_on_Polity_IV_scale.png/220px-Number_of_nations_1800-2003_scoring_8_or_higher_on_Polity_IV_scale.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Number_of_nations_1800-2003_scoring_8_or_higher_on_Polity_IV_scale.png/330px-Number_of_nations_1800-2003_scoring_8_or_higher_on_Polity_IV_scale.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Number_of_nations_1800-2003_scoring_8_or_higher_on_Polity_IV_scale.png/440px-Number_of_nations_1800-2003_scoring_8_or_higher_on_Polity_IV_scale.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Number_of_nations_1800-2003_scoring_8_or_higher_on_Polity_IV_scale.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The number of nations 1800–2003 scoring 8 or higher on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polity_IV" title="Polity IV">Polity IV</a> scale, another widely used measure of democracy</div></div></div>
<p>20th-century transitions to liberal democracy have come in successive "<a href="/wiki/Waves_of_democracy" title="Waves of democracy">waves of democracy</a>", variously resulting from wars, revolutions, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decolonisation" title="Decolonisation">decolonisation</a>, and religious and economic circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> Global waves of "democratic regression" reversing democratization, have also occurred in the 1920s and 30s, in the 1960s and 1970s, and in the 2010s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-124">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and the dissolution of the autocratic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_empire" title="Ottoman empire">Ottoman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austro-Hungarian</a> empires resulted in the creation of new nation-states in Europe, most of them at least nominally democratic. In the 1920s democratic movements flourished and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_women%27s_suffrage" title="Timeline of women's suffrage">women's suffrage</a> advanced, but the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> brought disenchantment and most of the countries of Europe, Latin America, and Asia turned to strong-man rule or dictatorships. <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">Fascism</a> and dictatorships flourished in <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, as well as non-democratic governments in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltics" title="Baltics">Baltics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> brought a definitive reversal of this trend in western Europe. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratisation" title="Democratisation">democratisation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Allied_Control_Council" title="Allied Control Council">American, British, and French sectors of occupied Germany</a> (disputed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup>), Austria, Italy, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occupied_Japan" title="Occupied Japan">occupied Japan</a> served as a model for the later theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_change" title="Government change">government change</a>. However, most of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Democratic_Republic" title="German Democratic Republic">Soviet sector of Germany</a> fell into the non-democratic <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Soviet-dominated bloc</a>.
</p><p>The war was followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decolonisation" title="Decolonisation">decolonisation</a>, and again most of the new independent states had nominally democratic constitutions.
