India emerged as the world's largest democracy and continues to be so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> Countries that were once part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> often adopted the British <a href="/wiki/Westminster_system" title="Westminster system">Westminster system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> By 1960, the vast majority of country-states were nominally democracies, although most of the world's populations lived in nominal democracies that experienced sham elections, and other forms of subterfuge (particularly in <a href="/wiki/Communist_state" title="Communist state">"Communist" states</a> and the former colonies.)
</p><p>A subsequent wave of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratisation" title="Democratisation">democratisation</a> brought substantial gains toward true liberal democracy for many states, dubbed "third wave of democracy." Portugal, Spain, and several of the military dictatorships in South America returned to civilian rule in the 1970s and 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[nb 2]</a></sup> This was followed by countries in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> by the mid-to-late 1980s. Economic malaise in the 1980s, along with resentment of Soviet oppression, contributed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Soviet_Union_(1985-1991)" title="History of the Soviet Union (1985-1991)">collapse of the Soviet Union</a>, the associated end of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, and the democratisation and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberalisation" title="Liberalisation">liberalisation</a> of the former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_bloc" title="Eastern bloc">Eastern bloc</a> countries. The most successful of the new democracies were those geographically and culturally closest to western Europe, and they are now either part of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> or <a href="/wiki/Potential_enlargement_of_the_European_Union" title="Potential enlargement of the European Union">candidate states</a>. In 1986, after the toppling of the most prominent Asian dictatorship, the only democratic state of its kind at the time emerged in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> with the rise of <a href="/wiki/Corazon_Aquino" title="Corazon Aquino">Corazon Aquino</a>, who would later be known as the Mother of Asian Democracy.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corazon_Aquino_inauguration.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="460" data-file-width="670" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Corazon_Aquino_inauguration.jpg/220px-Corazon_Aquino_inauguration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Corazon_Aquino_inauguration.jpg/330px-Corazon_Aquino_inauguration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Corazon_Aquino_inauguration.jpg/440px-Corazon_Aquino_inauguration.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corazon_Aquino_inauguration.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Corazon_Aquino" title="Corazon Aquino">Corazon Aquino</a> taking the Oath of Office, becoming the first female president in Asia</div></div></div>
<p>The liberal trend spread to some states in Africa in the 1990s, most prominently in South Africa. Some recent examples of attempts of liberalisation include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indonesian_Revolution_of_1998" title="Indonesian Revolution of 1998">Indonesian Revolution of 1998</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Overthrow_of_Slobodan_Milo%C5%A1evi%C4%87" title="Overthrow of Slobodan Milošević">Bulldozer Revolution</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Rose_Revolution" title="Rose Revolution">Rose Revolution</a> in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Orange_Revolution" title="Orange Revolution">Orange Revolution</a> in Ukraine, the <a href="/wiki/Cedar_Revolution" title="Cedar Revolution">Cedar Revolution</a> in Lebanon, the <a href="/wiki/Tulip_Revolution" title="Tulip Revolution">Tulip Revolution</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%932011_Tunisian_revolution" title="2010–2011 Tunisian revolution">Jasmine Revolution</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Age_of_democracies_at_the_end_of_2015,_OWID.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Age_of_democracies_at_the_end_of_2015%2C_OWID.svg/220px-Age_of_democracies_at_the_end_of_2015%2C_OWID.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Age_of_democracies_at_the_end_of_2015%2C_OWID.svg/330px-Age_of_democracies_at_the_end_of_2015%2C_OWID.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Age_of_democracies_at_the_end_of_2015%2C_OWID.svg/440px-Age_of_democracies_at_the_end_of_2015%2C_OWID.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Age_of_democracies_at_the_end_of_2015,_OWID.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Age of democracies at the end of 2015<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a>, in 2007 there were 123 electoral democracies (up from 40 in 1972).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup> According to <i>World Forum on Democracy</i>, electoral democracies now represent 120 of the 192 existing countries and constitute 58.2 percent of the world's population. At the same time liberal democracies i.e. countries Freedom House regards as free and respectful of basic human rights and the rule of law are 85 in number and represent 38 percent of the global population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> Also in 2007 the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> declared 15 September the <a href="/wiki/International_Day_of_Democracy" title="International Day of Democracy">International Day of Democracy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nordiska_radets_presidium_haller_mote_med_de_nordiska_statsministrarna_under_session_i_Helsingfors_2008-10-28.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Nordiska_radets_presidium_haller_mote_med_de_nordiska_statsministrarna_under_session_i_Helsingfors_2008-10-28.jpg/220px-Nordiska_radets_presidium_haller_mote_med_de_nordiska_statsministrarna_under_session_i_Helsingfors_2008-10-28.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Nordiska_radets_presidium_haller_mote_med_de_nordiska_statsministrarna_under_session_i_Helsingfors_2008-10-28.jpg/330px-Nordiska_radets_presidium_haller_mote_med_de_nordiska_statsministrarna_under_session_i_Helsingfors_2008-10-28.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Nordiska_radets_presidium_haller_mote_med_de_nordiska_statsministrarna_under_session_i_Helsingfors_2008-10-28.jpg/440px-Nordiska_radets_presidium_haller_mote_med_de_nordiska_statsministrarna_under_session_i_Helsingfors_2008-10-28.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nordiska_radets_presidium_haller_mote_med_de_nordiska_statsministrarna_under_session_i_Helsingfors_2008-10-28.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Meeting of the Grand Committee of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Finland" title="Parliament of Finland">Parliament of Finland</a> in 2008</div></div></div>
<p>Most electoral democracies continue to exclude those younger than 18 from voting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup> The voting age has been lowered to 16 for national elections in a number of countries, including Brazil, Austria, Cuba, and Nicaragua. In California, a 2004 proposal to permit a quarter vote at 14 and a half vote at 16 was ultimately defeated. In 2008, the German parliament proposed but shelved a bill that would grant the vote to each citizen at birth, to be used by a parent until the child claims it for themselves.
</p><p>According to Freedom House, starting in 2005, there have been eleven consecutive years in which declines in political rights and civil liberties throughout the world have outnumbered improvements,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-137">[135]</a></sup> as <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalist</a> political forces have gained ground everywhere from Poland (under the <a href="/wiki/Law_and_Justice" title="Law and Justice">Law and Justice Party</a>) to the Philippines (under <a href="/wiki/Rodrigo_Duterte" title="Rodrigo Duterte">Rodrigo Duterte</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-137">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-124">[123]</a></sup> In a Freedom House report released in 2018, Democracy Scores for most countries declined for the 12th consecutive year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Christian_Science_Monitor" title="The Christian Science Monitor">The Christian Science Monitor</a></i> reported that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalist" title="Nationalist">nationalist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Populist" title="Populist">populist</a> political ideologies were gaining ground, at the expense of <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a>, in countries like Poland, Turkey and Hungary. For example, in Poland, the President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2015%E2%80%93present_Polish_constitutional_crisis" title="2015–present Polish constitutional crisis">appointed 27 new Supreme Court judges</a> over legal objections from the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>. In Turkey, thousands of judges were removed from their positions following a <a href="/wiki/2016_Turkish_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="2016 Turkish coup d'état attempt">failed coup attempt</a> during a <a href="/wiki/2016%E2%80%93present_purges_in_Turkey" title="2016–present purges in Turkey">government crackdown</a> .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Countries_democratizing_or_autocratizing_substantially_and_significantly_2010%E2%80%932020.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="2754" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Countries_democratizing_or_autocratizing_substantially_and_significantly_2010%E2%80%932020.svg/220px-Countries_democratizing_or_autocratizing_substantially_and_significantly_2010%E2%80%932020.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Countries_democratizing_or_autocratizing_substantially_and_significantly_2010%E2%80%932020.svg/330px-Countries_democratizing_or_autocratizing_substantially_and_significantly_2010%E2%80%932020.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Countries_democratizing_or_autocratizing_substantially_and_significantly_2010%E2%80%932020.svg/440px-Countries_democratizing_or_autocratizing_substantially_and_significantly_2010%E2%80%932020.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Countries_democratizing_or_autocratizing_substantially_and_significantly_2010%E2%80%932020.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries autocratizing (red)  or democratizing (blue) substantially and significantly (2010–2020). Countries in grey are substantially unchanged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-140">[138]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Democratic_backsliding" title="Democratic backsliding">Democratic backsliding</a>" in the 2010s were attributed to economic inequality and social discontent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup> personalism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup> poor management of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impact_of_the_COVID-19_pandemic_on_politics" title="Impact of the COVID-19 pandemic on politics">COVID-19 pandemic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup>  as well as other factors such as government manipulation of civil society, "toxic polarization," foreign disinformation campaigns,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2019VDem_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-2019VDem-145">[143]</a></sup> racism and nativism,  excessive executive power,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup> and decreased power of the opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup> Within English-speaking Western democracies, "protection-based" attitudes combining cultural conservatism and leftist economic attitudes were the strongest predictor of support for authoritarian modes of governance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory">Theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_theory">Early theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Early theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> contrasted rule by the many (democracy/<a href="/wiki/Timocracy" title="Timocracy">timocracy</a>), with rule by the few (<a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a>/<a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a>), and with rule by a single person (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyranny" title="Tyranny">tyranny</a> or today <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocracy</a>/<a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a>). He also thought that there was a good and a bad variant of each system (he considered democracy to be the degenerate counterpart to timocracy).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>A common view among early and renaissance <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">Republican</a> theorists was that democracy could only survive in small political communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-153">[151]</a></sup> Heeding the lessons of the Roman Republic's shift to monarchism as it grew larger or smaller, these Republican theorists held that the expansion of territory and population inevitably led to tyranny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-153">[151]</a></sup> Democracy was therefore highly fragile and rare historically, as it could only survive in small political units, which due to their size were vulnerable to conquest by larger political units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-153">[151]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a> famously said, "if a republic is small, it is destroyed by an outside force; if it is large, it is destroyed by an internal vice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_153-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-153">[151]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Rousseau</a> asserted, "It is, therefore the natural property of small states to be governed as a republic, of middling ones to be subject to a monarch, and of large empires to be swayed by a despotic prince."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_153-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-153">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_theory">Contemporary theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Contemporary theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Among modern political theorists, there are three contending conceptions of democracy: <i>aggregative democracy</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Deliberative_democracy" title="Deliberative democracy">deliberative democracy</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Radical_democracy" title="Radical democracy">radical democracy</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aggregative">Aggregative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Aggregative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The theory of <i>aggregative democracy</i> claims that the aim of the democratic processes is to solicit citizens' preferences and aggregate them together to determine what social policies society should adopt. Therefore, proponents of this view hold that democratic participation should primarily focus on <a href="/wiki/Voting" title="Voting">voting</a>, where the policy with the most votes gets implemented.
</p><p>Different variants of aggregative democracy exist. Under <i>minimalism</i>, democracy is a system of government in which citizens have given teams of political leaders the right to rule in periodic elections. According to this minimalist conception, citizens cannot and should not "rule" because, for example, on most issues, most of the time, they have no clear views or their views are not well-founded. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Schumpeter" title="Joseph Schumpeter">Joseph Schumpeter</a> articulated this view most famously in his book <i>Capitalism, Socialism, and Democracy</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup> Contemporary proponents of minimalism include <a href="/wiki/William_H._Riker" title="William H. Riker">William H. Riker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adam_Przeworski" title="Adam Przeworski">Adam Przeworski</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Posner" title="Richard Posner">Richard Posner</a>.
</p><p>According to the theory of <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a>, on the other hand, citizens should vote directly, not through their representatives, on legislative proposals. Proponents of direct democracy offer varied reasons to support this view. Political activity can be valuable in itself, it socialises and educates citizens, and popular participation can check powerful elites. Most importantly, citizens do not rule themselves unless they directly decide laws and policies.
</p><p>Governments will tend to produce laws and policies that are <a href="/wiki/Median_voter_theorem" title="Median voter theorem">close to the views of the median voter</a>—with half to their left and the other half to their right. This is not a desirable outcome as it represents the action of self-interested and somewhat unaccountable political elites competing for votes. <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Downs" title="Anthony Downs">Anthony Downs</a> suggests that ideological political parties are necessary to act as a mediating broker between individual and governments. Downs laid out this view in his 1957 book <i>An Economic Theory of Democracy</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_A._Dahl" title="Robert A. Dahl">Robert A. Dahl</a> argues that the fundamental democratic principle is that, when it comes to binding collective decisions, each person in a political community is entitled to have his/her interests be given equal consideration (not necessarily that all people are equally satisfied by the collective decision). He uses the term <a href="/wiki/Polyarchy" title="Polyarchy">polyarchy</a> to refer to societies in which there exists a certain set of institutions and procedures which are perceived as leading to such democracy. First and foremost among these institutions is the regular occurrence of free and open <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elections" title="Elections">elections</a> which are used to select representatives who then manage all or most of the public policy of the society. However, these polyarchic procedures may not create a full democracy if, for example, poverty prevents political participation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Dworkin" title="Ronald Dworkin">Ronald Dworkin</a> argues that "democracy is a substantive, not a merely procedural, ideal."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deliberative">Deliberative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Deliberative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Deliberative_democracy" title="Deliberative democracy">Deliberative democracy</a></i> is based on the notion that democracy is government by <a href="/wiki/Deliberation" title="Deliberation">deliberation</a>. Unlike aggregative democracy, deliberative democracy holds that, for a democratic decision to be legitimate, it must be preceded by authentic deliberation, not merely the aggregation of preferences that occurs in voting. <i>Authentic deliberation</i> is deliberation among decision-makers that is free from distortions of unequal political power, such as power a decision-maker obtained through economic wealth or the support of interest groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[159]</a></sup> If the decision-makers cannot reach <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consensus" title="Consensus">consensus</a> after authentically deliberating on a proposal, then they vote on the proposal using a form of majority rule. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citizens%27_assemblies" title="Citizens' assemblies">Citizens assemblies</a> are considered by many scholars as practical examples of deliberative democracy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup> with a recent <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> report identifying citizens assemblies as an increasingly popular mechanism to involve citizens in governmental decision-making.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radical">Radical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Radical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Radical_democracy" title="Radical democracy">Radical democracy</a></i> is based on the idea that there are hierarchical and oppressive power relations that exist in society. Democracy's role is to make visible and challenge those relations by allowing for difference, dissent and antagonisms in decision-making processes.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement_of_democracy">Measurement of democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Measurement of democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indices_ranking_degree_of_democracy">Indices ranking degree of democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Indices ranking degree of democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_indices" title="Freedom indices">freedom indices</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:432px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electoral_democracies.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="2754" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Electoral_democracies.svg/430px-Electoral_democracies.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Electoral_democracies.svg/645px-Electoral_democracies.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Electoral_democracies.svg/860px-Electoral_democracies.svg.png 2x" width="430"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electoral_democracies.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#000080; color:white;"> </span> Countries</span> designated "<a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">electoral democracies</a>" in Freedom House's <i>Freedom in the World 2021</i> survey, covering the year 2020<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[164]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Ranking of the degree of democracy are published by several organisations according to their own various definitions of the term and relying on different types of data:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/V-Dem_Institute" title="V-Dem Institute">V-Dem Institute</a>'s <i>Varieties of Democracy Report</i> is published each year since 2014 by the Swedish research institute V-Dem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-140">[138]</a></sup> It includes separate indices measuring five different types of democracy: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electoral_democracy" title="Electoral democracy">electoral democracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Participatory_democracy" title="Participatory democracy">participatory democracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deliberative_democracy" title="Deliberative democracy">deliberative democracy</a>, and egalitarian democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Democracy_Index" title="Democracy Index">Democracy Index</a></i>, published by the UK-based <a href="/wiki/Economist_Intelligence_Unit" title="Economist Intelligence Unit">Economist Intelligence Unit</a>, is an assessment of countries' democracy. Countries are rated to be either <i>Full Democracies</i>, <i>Flawed Democracies</i>, <i>Hybrid Regimes</i>, or <i>Authoritarian regimes</i>. Full democracies, flawed democracies, and hybrid regimes are considered to be democracies, and the authoritarian states are considered to be dictatorial or <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchic</a>. The index is based on 60 indicators grouped in five different categories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup></li>
<li>The U.S.-based <i><a href="/wiki/Polity_data_series" title="Polity data series">Polity data series</a></i> is a widely used data series in political science research. It contains coded annual information on regime authority characteristics and transitions for all independent states with greater than 500,000 total population and covers the years 1800–2006. Polity's conclusions about a state's level of democracy are based on an evaluation of that state's elections for competitiveness, openness and level of participation. The Polity work is sponsored by the <a href="/wiki/Political_Instability_Task_Force" title="Political Instability Task Force">Political Instability Task Force</a> (PITF) which is funded by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a>. However, the views expressed in the reports are the authors' alone and do not represent the views of the US Government.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MaxRange" title="MaxRange">MaxRange</a>, a dataset defining level of democracy and institutional structure(regime-type) on a 100-graded scale where every value represents a unique regime type. Values are sorted from 1–100 based on level of democracy and political accountability. MaxRange defines the value corresponding to all states and every month from 1789 to 2015 and updating. MaxRange is created and developed by Max Range, and is now associated with the university of Halmstad, Sweden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Other indices measuring freedom and human rights include degree of democracy as an element. Some of these include the <i><a href="/wiki/Freedom_in_the_World" title="Freedom in the World">Freedom in the World</a></i> ranking, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Worldwide_Press_Freedom_Index" title="Worldwide Press Freedom Index">Worldwide Press Freedom Index</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Index_of_Freedom_in_the_World" title="Index of Freedom in the World">Index of Freedom in the World</a></i>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/CIRI_Human_Rights_Data_Project" title="CIRI Human Rights Data Project">CIRI Human Rights Data Project</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Difficulties_in_measuring_democracy">Difficulties in measuring democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Difficulties in measuring democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because democracy is an overarching concept that includes the functioning of diverse institutions which are not easy to measure, strong limitations exist in quantifying and econometrically measuring the potential effects of democracy or its relationship with other phenomena—whether inequality, poverty, education etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_of_Economic_Methodology_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Journal_of_Economic_Methodology-172">[170]</a></sup> Given the constraints in acquiring reliable data with within-country variation on aspects of democracy, academics have largely studied cross-country variations. Yet variations between democratic institutions are very large across countries which constrains meaningful comparisons using statistical approaches. Since democracy is typically measured aggregately as a macro variable using a single observation for each country and each year, studying democracy faces a range of econometric constraints and is limited to basic correlations. Cross-country comparison of a composite, comprehensive and qualitative concept like democracy may thus not always be, for many purposes, methodologically rigorous or useful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Journal_of_Economic_Methodology_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-Journal_of_Economic_Methodology-172">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dieter Fuchs and Edeltraud Roller suggest that, in order to truly measure the quality of democracy, objective measurements need to be complemented by "subjective measurements based on the perspective of citizens".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[171]</a></sup> Similarly, Quinton Mayne and Brigitte Geißel also defend that the quality of democracy does not depend exclusively on the performance of institutions, but also on the citizens' own dispositions and commitment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_governmental_democracies">Types of governmental democracies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Types of governmental democracies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Types_of_democracy" title="Types of democracy">Types of democracy</a></div>
<p>Democracy has taken a number of forms, both in theory and practice. Some varieties of democracy provide better representation and more freedom for their citizens than others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[174]</a></sup> However, if any democracy is not structured to prohibit the government from excluding the people from the legislative process, or any branch of government from altering the <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation of powers</a> in its favour, then a branch of the system can accumulate too much power and destroy the democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Forms_of_government_2021.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1399" data-file-width="2756" decoding="async" height="381" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Forms_of_government_2021.svg/750px-Forms_of_government_2021.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Forms_of_government_2021.svg/1125px-Forms_of_government_2021.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Forms_of_government_2021.svg/1500px-Forms_of_government_2021.svg.png 2x" width="750"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Forms_of_government_2021.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div style="text-align:center"><span style="font-size:small;">World's states coloured by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Form_of_government" title="Form of government">form of government</a><sup>1</sup></span></div> <table align="center" style="width:100%; This map presents only the <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> form of government, and not the <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> degree of democracy.</div></div></div></div>
<p>The following kinds of democracy are not exclusive of one another: many specify details of aspects that are independent of one another and can co-exist in a single system.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_forms">Basic forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Basic forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several variants of democracy exist, but there are two basic forms, both of which concern how the whole body of all eligible citizens executes its will. One form of democracy is <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a>, in which all eligible citizens have active participation in the political decision making, for example voting on policy initiatives directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[178]</a></sup> In most modern democracies, the whole body of eligible citizens remain the sovereign power but political power is exercised indirectly through elected representatives; this is called a <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democracy</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct">Direct</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Direct">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">Direct democracy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Landsgemeinde_Glarus,_2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Landsgemeinde_Glarus%2C_2009.jpg/220px-Landsgemeinde_Glarus%2C_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Landsgemeinde_Glarus%2C_2009.jpg/330px-Landsgemeinde_Glarus%2C_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Landsgemeinde_Glarus%2C_2009.jpg/440px-Landsgemeinde_Glarus%2C_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Landsgemeinde_Glarus,_2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Landsgemeinde" title="Landsgemeinde">Landsgemeinde</a> (in 2009) of the <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Glarus" title="Canton of Glarus">canton of Glarus</a>, an example of direct democracy in Switzerland</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Swiss_voting_material.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1832" data-file-width="2792" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Swiss_voting_material.jpg/220px-Swiss_voting_material.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Swiss_voting_material.jpg/330px-Swiss_voting_material.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Swiss_voting_material.jpg/440px-Swiss_voting_material.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Swiss_voting_material.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In <a href="/wiki/Voting_in_Switzerland" title="Voting in Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, without needing to register, every citizen receives <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ballot_papers" title="Ballot papers">ballot papers</a> and information brochures for each vote (and can send it back by post). Switzerland has a <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a> system and votes (and elections) are organised about four times a year; here, to <a href="/wiki/Bern#Politics" title="Bern">Berne</a>'s citizen in November 2008 about 5 national, 2 cantonal, 4 municipal referendums, and 2 elections (government and parliament of the City of Berne) to take care of at the same time.</div></div></div>
<p>Direct democracy is a political system where the citizens participate in the decision-making personally, contrary to relying on intermediaries or representatives.  A direct democracy gives the voting population the power to:
</p>
<ol><li>Change constitutional laws,</li>
<li>Put forth <a href="/wiki/Initiative" title="Initiative">initiatives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendums</a> and suggestions for laws,</li>
<li>Give binding orders to elective officials, such as revoking them before the end of their elected term or initiating a lawsuit for breaking a campaign promise.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Where is direct democracy characterized in this way? (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ol>
<p>Within modern-day representative governments, certain electoral tools like referendums, citizens' initiatives and <a href="/wiki/Recall_election" title="Recall election">recall elections</a> are referred to as forms of direct democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[179]</a></sup> However, some advocates of direct democracy argue for local assemblies of face-to-face discussion. Direct democracy as a government system currently exists in the <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss</a> <a href="/wiki/Cantons_of_Switzerland" title="Cantons of Switzerland">cantons</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canton_of_Appenzell_Innerrhoden" title="Canton of Appenzell Innerrhoden">Appenzell Innerrhoden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Glarus" title="Canton of Glarus">Glarus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golay_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Golay-182">[180]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Rebel_Zapatista_Autonomous_Municipalities" title="Rebel Zapatista Autonomous Municipalities">Rebel Zapatista Autonomous Municipalities</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[181]</a></sup> communities affiliated with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIPO-RFM" title="CIPO-RFM">CIPO-RFM</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[182]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivian</a> city councils of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fejuve" title="Fejuve">FEJUVE</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup> and Kurdish cantons of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rojava" title="Rojava">Rojava</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Lot_system">Lot system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Lot system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The use of a lot system, a characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Athenian_democracy" title="Athenian democracy">Athenian democracy</a>, is a feature of some versions of direct democracies. In this system, important governmental and administrative tasks are performed by citizens picked from a lottery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manin_1997_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manin_1997-187">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Representative">Representative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Representative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">Representative democracy</a></div>
<p>Representative democracy involves the election of government officials by the people being represented. If the head of state is also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratically_elected_government" title="Democratically elected government">democratically elected</a> then it is called a democratic <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[186]</a></sup> The most common mechanisms involve election of the candidate with a majority or a <a href="/wiki/Plurality_(voting)" title="Plurality (voting)">plurality</a> of the votes. Most western countries have representative systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golay_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-Golay-182">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>Representatives may be elected or become diplomatic representatives by a particular district (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constituency" title="Constituency">constituency</a>), or represent the entire electorate through <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional</a> systems, with some using a combination of the two. Some representative democracies also incorporate elements of direct democracy, such as <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendums</a>. A characteristic of representative democracy is that while the representatives are elected by the people to act in the people's interest, they retain the freedom to exercise their own judgement as how best to do so. Such reasons have driven criticism upon representative democracy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[188]</a></sup> pointing out the contradictions of representation mechanisms with democracy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliamentary">Parliamentary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Parliamentary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">Parliamentary system</a></div>
<p>Parliamentary democracy is a representative democracy where government is appointed by or can be dismissed by, representatives as opposed to a "presidential rule" wherein the president is both head of state and the head of government and is elected by the voters. Under a parliamentary democracy, government is exercised by delegation to an executive ministry and subject to ongoing review, checks and balances by the legislative parliament elected by the people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a parliamentary system, the Prime Minister may be dismissed by the legislature at any point in time for not meeting the expectations of the legislature. This is done through a Vote of No Confidence where the legislature decides whether or not to remove the Prime Minister from office with majority support for dismissal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neil,_Patrick_H_2010_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neil,_Patrick_H_2010-197">[195]</a></sup> In some countries, the Prime Minister can also call an election at any point in time, typically when the Prime Minister believes that they are in good favour with the public as to get re-elected. In other parliamentary democracies, extra elections are virtually never held, a minority government being preferred until the next ordinary elections. An important feature of the parliamentary democracy is the concept of the "<a href="/wiki/Loyal_opposition" title="Loyal opposition">loyal opposition</a>". The essence of the concept is that the second largest political party (or coalition) opposes the governing party (or coalition), while still remaining loyal to the state and its democratic principles.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential">Presidential</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Presidential">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">Presidential system</a></div>
<p>Presidential Democracy is a system where the public elects the president through an election. The president serves as both the head of state and head of government controlling most of the executive powers. The president serves for a specific term and cannot exceed that amount of time. The legislature often has limited ability to remove a president from office. Elections typically have a fixed date and aren't easily changed. The president has direct control over the cabinet, specifically appointing the cabinet members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neil,_Patrick_H_2010_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neil,_Patrick_H_2010-197">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>The executive usually has the responsibility to execute or implement legislation and may have the limited legislative powers, such as a veto. However, a legislative branch passes legislation and budgets. This provides some measure of <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation of powers</a>. In consequence, however, the president and the legislature may end up in the control of separate parties, allowing one to block the other and thereby interfere with the orderly operation of the state. This may be the reason why presidential democracy is not very common outside the Americas, Africa, and Central and Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neil,_Patrick_H_2010_197-2"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neil,_Patrick_H_2010-197">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-presidential_system" title="Semi-presidential system">semi-presidential system</a> is a system of democracy in which the government includes both a prime minister and a president. The particular powers held by the prime minister and president vary by country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neil,_Patrick_H_2010_197-3"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neil,_Patrick_H_2010-197">[195]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrid_or_semi-direct">Hybrid or semi-direct</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Hybrid or semi-direct">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Switzerland" title="Politics of Switzerland">Politics of Switzerland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voting_in_Switzerland" title="Voting in Switzerland">Voting in Switzerland</a></div>
<p>Some modern democracies that are predominantly representative in nature also heavily rely upon forms of political action that are directly democratic. These democracies, which combine elements of representative democracy and direct democracy, are termed <i>hybrid democracies</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[196]</a></sup> <i>semi-direct democracies</i> or <i>participatory democracies</i>. Examples include Switzerland and some <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. states</a>, where frequent use is made of <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendums</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Initiatives" title="Initiatives">initiatives</a>.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiss_confederation" title="Swiss confederation">Swiss confederation</a> is a semi-direct democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golay_182-2"><a href="#cite_note-Golay-182">[180]</a></sup> At the federal level, citizens can propose changes to the constitution (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_popular_initiative" title="Federal popular initiative">federal popular initiative</a>) or ask for a <a href="/wiki/Optional_referendum" title="Optional referendum">referendum</a> to be held on any law voted by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Assembly_(Switzerland)" title="Federal Assembly (Switzerland)">parliament</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golay_182-3"><a href="#cite_note-Golay-182">[180]</a></sup> Between January 1995 and June 2005, Swiss citizens voted 31 times, to answer 103 questions (during the same period, French citizens participated in only two referendums).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golay_182-4"><a href="#cite_note-Golay-182">[180]</a></sup> Although in the past 120 years less than 250 initiatives have been put to referendum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>Examples include the extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendums</a> in the US state of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, which is a state that has more than 20 million voters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Town_meeting" title="Town meeting">town meetings</a> are often used, especially in rural areas, to manage local government. This creates a hybrid form of government, with a local <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a> and a representative state government. For example, most <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a> towns hold annual town meetings in March in which town officers are elected, budgets for the town and schools are voted on, and citizens have the opportunity to speak and be heard on political matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitutional_monarchy">Constitutional monarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Constitutional monarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">Constitutional monarchy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_II_greets_NASA_GSFC_employees,_May_8,_2007_edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2060" data-file-width="1488" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Elizabeth_II_greets_NASA_GSFC_employees%2C_May_8%2C_2007_edit.jpg/170px-Elizabeth_II_greets_NASA_GSFC_employees%2C_May_8%2C_2007_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Elizabeth_II_greets_NASA_GSFC_employees%2C_May_8%2C_2007_edit.jpg/255px-Elizabeth_II_greets_NASA_GSFC_employees%2C_May_8%2C_2007_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Elizabeth_II_greets_NASA_GSFC_employees%2C_May_8%2C_2007_edit.jpg/340px-Elizabeth_II_greets_NASA_GSFC_employees%2C_May_8%2C_2007_edit.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_II_greets_NASA_GSFC_employees,_May_8,_2007_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a>, a constitutional monarch</div></div></div>
<p>Many countries such as the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian countries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a> turned powerful monarchs into constitutional monarchs with limited or, often gradually, merely symbolic roles. For example, in the predecessor states to the United Kingdom, constitutional monarchy began to emerge and has continued uninterrupted since the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> of 1688 and passage of the <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights 1689</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kopstein2014_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kopstein2014-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-refNARoP_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-refNARoP-78">[78]</a></sup> Strongly limited constitutional monarchies, such as the United Kingdom, have been referred to as <a href="/wiki/Crowned_republic" title="Crowned republic">crowned republics</a> by writers such as <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[200]</a></sup>
</p><p>In other countries, the monarchy was abolished along with the aristocratic system (as in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>). An elected person, with or without significant powers, became the head of state in these countries.
</p><p>Elite upper houses of legislatures, which often had lifetime or hereditary tenure, were common in many states. Over time, these either had their powers limited (as with the British <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>) or else became elective and remained powerful (as with the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Senate" title="Australian Senate">Australian Senate</a>).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Republic">Republic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Republic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">Republicanism</a></div>
<p>The term <i><a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a></i> has many different meanings, but today often refers to a representative democracy with an elected <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a>, such as a <a href="/wiki/President_(government_title)" title="President (government title)">president</a>, serving for a limited term, in contrast to states with a hereditary <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarch</a> as a head of state, even if these states also are representative democracies with an elected or appointed <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a> such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_Minister" title="Prime Minister">prime minister</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers of the United States</a> rarely praised and often criticised <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a> (often simply calling it, without distinction, "democracy"), which in their view often came without the protection of a constitution enshrining inalienable rights; <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> argued, especially in <a href="/wiki/Federalist_No._10" title="Federalist No. 10"><i>The Federalist</i> No. 10</a>, that what distinguished a direct <i>democracy</i> from a <i>republic</i> was that the former became weaker as it got larger and suffered more violently from the effects of faction, whereas a republic could get stronger as it got larger and combats faction by its very structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Federalist10_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Federalist10-204">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>Professors Richard Ellis of <a href="/wiki/Willamette_University" title="Willamette University">Willamette University</a> and Michael Nelson of <a href="/wiki/Rhodes_College" title="Rhodes College">Rhodes College</a> argue that much constitutional thought, from Madison to Lincoln and beyond, has focused on "the problem of majority tyranny." They conclude, "The principles of republican government embedded in the Constitution represent an effort by the framers to ensure that the inalienable rights of life, liberty, and the pursuit of happiness would not be trampled by majorities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[203]</a></sup> What was critical to American values, <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> insisted,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[204]</a></sup> was that the government be "bound by fixed laws, which the people have a voice in making, and a right to defend." As Benjamin Franklin was exiting after writing the U.S. constitution, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Willing_Powel" title="Elizabeth Willing Powel">Elizabeth Willing Powel</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brockell_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brockell-207">[205]</a></sup> asked him "Well, Doctor, what have we got—a republic or a monarchy?". He replied "A republic—if you can keep it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[206]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberal_democracy">Liberal democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Liberal democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">Liberal democracy</a></div>
<p>A liberal democracy is a representative democracy in which the ability of the elected representatives to exercise decision-making power is subject to the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a>, and moderated by a constitution or laws that emphasise the protection of the rights and freedoms of individuals, and which places constraints on the leaders and on the extent to which the will of the majority can be exercised against the rights of minorities (see <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberties</a>).
</p><p>In a liberal democracy, it is possible for some large-scale decisions to <a href="/wiki/Emergent_democracy" title="Emergent democracy">emerge</a> from the many individual decisions that citizens are free to make. In other words, citizens can "vote with their feet" or "vote with their dollars", resulting in significant informal government-by-the-masses that exercises many "powers" associated with formal government elsewhere.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Socialist">Socialist</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Socialist">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Democracy_in_Marxism" title="Democracy in Marxism">Democracy in Marxism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">Socialist</a> thought has several different views on democracy. <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">Social democracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">democratic socialism</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship_of_the_proletariat" title="Dictatorship of the proletariat">dictatorship of the proletariat</a> (usually exercised through <a href="/wiki/Soviet_democracy" title="Soviet democracy">Soviet democracy</a>) are some examples. Many democratic socialists and social democrats believe in a form of <a href="/wiki/Participatory_democracy" title="Participatory democracy">participatory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Industrial_democracy" title="Industrial democracy">industrial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economic_democracy" title="Economic democracy">economic</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Workplace_democracy" title="Workplace democracy">workplace democracy</a> combined with a <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democracy</a>.
</p><p>Within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democracy_in_Marxist_theory" title="Democracy in Marxist theory">Marxist orthodoxy</a> there is a hostility to what is commonly called "liberal democracy", which is simply referred to as parliamentary democracy because of its often centralised nature. Because of orthodox Marxists' desire to eliminate the political elitism they see in capitalism, <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leninism" title="Leninism">Leninists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trotskyism" title="Trotskyism">Trotskyists</a> believe in direct democracy implemented through a system of communes (which are sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Soviet_(council)" title="Soviet (council)">soviets</a>). This system ultimately manifests itself as council democracy and begins with workplace democracy.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Democracy cannot consist solely of elections that are nearly always fictitious and managed by rich landowners and professional politicians.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Che_Guevara" title="Che Guevara">Che Guevara</a>, speech in Uruguay, 1961<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[207]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anarchist">Anarchist</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Anarchist">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">Anarchists</a> are split in this domain, depending on whether they believe that a <a href="/wiki/Tyranny_of_the_majority" title="Tyranny of the majority">majority-rule is tyrannic or not</a>.  To many anarchists, the only form of democracy considered acceptable is direct democracy. <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Joseph_Proudhon" title="Pierre-Joseph Proudhon">Pierre-Joseph Proudhon</a> argued that the only acceptable form of direct democracy is one in which it is recognised that majority decisions are not binding on the minority, even when unanimous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[208]</a></sup> However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarcho-communist" title="Anarcho-communist">anarcho-communist</a> <a href="/wiki/Murray_Bookchin" title="Murray Bookchin">Murray Bookchin</a> criticised <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Individualist_anarchists" title="Individualist anarchists">individualist anarchists</a> for opposing democracy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[209]</a></sup> and says "majority rule" is consistent with anarchism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some anarcho-communists oppose the majoritarian nature of direct democracy, feeling that it can impede individual liberty and opt-in favour of a non-majoritarian form of <a href="/wiki/Consensus_democracy" title="Consensus democracy">consensus democracy</a>, similar to Proudhon's position on direct democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[211]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a>, who did not self-identify as an anarchist but argued for "a better government"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[212]</a></sup> and is cited as an inspiration by some anarchists, argued that people should not be in the position of ruling others or being ruled when there is no consent.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sortition">Sortition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Sortition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sortition" title="Sortition">Sortition</a></div>
<p>Sometimes called "democracy without elections", <a href="/wiki/Sortition" title="Sortition">sortition</a> chooses decision makers via a random process. The intention is that those chosen will be representative of the opinions and interests of the people at large, and be more fair and impartial than an elected official. The technique was in widespread use in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athenian_Democracy" title="Athenian Democracy">Athenian Democracy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Florence" title="Republic of Florence">Renaissance Florence</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[213]</a></sup> and is still used in modern <a href="/wiki/Jury_selection" title="Jury selection">jury selection</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Consociational">Consociational</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Consociational">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consociational_democracy" title="Consociational democracy">Consociational democracy</a></div>
<p>A consociational democracy allows for simultaneous majority votes in two or more ethno-religious constituencies, and policies are enacted only if they gain majority support from both or all of them.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Consensus_democracy">Consensus democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Consensus democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Consensus_democracy" title="Consensus democracy">Consensus democracy</a></div>
<p>A consensus democracy, in contrast, would not be dichotomous. Instead, decisions would be based on a multi-option approach, and policies would be enacted if they gained sufficient support, either in a purely verbal agreement or via a consensus vote—a multi-option preference vote. If the threshold of support were at a sufficiently high level, minorities would be as it were protected automatically. Furthermore, any voting would be ethno-colour blind.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Supranational">Supranational</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Supranational">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qualified_majority_voting" title="Qualified majority voting">Qualified majority voting</a> is designed by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Rome" title="Treaty of Rome">Treaty of Rome</a> to be the principal method of reaching decisions in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Council_of_Ministers" title="European Council of Ministers">European Council of Ministers</a>. This system allocates votes to member states in part according to their population, but heavily weighted in favour of the smaller states. This might be seen as a form of representative democracy, but representatives to the Council might be appointed rather than directly elected.
</p>
