<div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><img alt="" data-file-height="125" data-file-width="120" decoding="async" height="27" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/HumanRightsLogo.svg/26px-HumanRightsLogo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/HumanRightsLogo.svg/39px-HumanRightsLogo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/HumanRightsLogo.svg/52px-HumanRightsLogo.svg.png 2x" width="26"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:Youth_rights" title="Category:Youth rights">Youth rights</a></li><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Social_sciences.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="122" data-file-width="139" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Social_sciences.svg/32px-Social_sciences.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Social_sciences.svg/48px-Social_sciences.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Social_sciences.svg/64px-Social_sciences.svg.png 2x" width="32"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Society" title="Portal:Society">Society portal</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Youth_rights_sidebar" title="Template:Youth rights sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Youth_rights_sidebar" title="Template talk:Youth rights sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Youth_rights_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclusive_democracy" title="Inclusive democracy">Inclusive democracy</a></div>
<p>Inclusive democracy is a political theory and political project that aims for <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a> in all fields of social life: political democracy in the form of face-to-face assemblies which are confederated, <a href="/wiki/Economic_democracy" title="Economic democracy">economic democracy</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Stateless_society" title="Stateless society">stateless</a>, moneyless and marketless economy, democracy in the social realm, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_self-management" title="Workers' self-management">self-management</a> in places of work and education, and ecological democracy which aims to reintegrate society and nature. The theoretical project of inclusive democracy emerged from the work of political philosopher <a href="/wiki/Takis_Fotopoulos" title="Takis Fotopoulos">Takis Fotopoulos</a> in "Towards An Inclusive Democracy" and was further developed in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Democracy_%26_Nature" title="Democracy &amp; Nature">Democracy &amp; Nature</a></i> and its successor <i>The International Journal of Inclusive Democracy</i>.
</p><p>The basic unit of decision making in an inclusive democracy is the demotic assembly, i.e. the assembly of demos, the citizen body in a given geographical area which may encompass a town and the surrounding villages, or even neighbourhoods of large cities. An inclusive democracy today can only take the form of a confederal democracy that is based on a network of administrative councils whose members or delegates are elected from popular face-to-face democratic assemblies in the various demoi. Thus, their role is purely administrative and practical, not one of policy-making like that of representatives in representative democracy.
</p><p>The citizen body is advised by experts but it is the citizen body which functions as the ultimate decision-taker. Authority can be delegated to a segment of the citizen body to carry out specific duties, for example, to serve as members of popular courts, or of regional and confederal councils. Such delegation is made, in principle, by lot, on a rotation basis, and is always recallable by the citizen body. Delegates to regional and confederal bodies should have specific mandates.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Participatory_politics">Participatory politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Participatory politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Participatory_politics" title="Participatory politics">Participatory politics</a></div>
<p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parpolity" title="Parpolity">Parpolity</a> or Participatory Polity is a theoretical form of democracy that is ruled by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parpolity" title="Parpolity">Nested Council</a> structure. The guiding philosophy is that people should have decision-making power in proportion to how much they are affected by the decision. Local councils of 25–50 people are completely autonomous on issues that affect only them, and these councils send delegates to higher level councils who are again autonomous regarding issues that affect only the population affected by that council.
</p><p>A council court of randomly chosen citizens serves as a check on the <a href="/wiki/Tyranny_of_the_majority" title="Tyranny of the majority">tyranny of the majority</a>, and rules on which body gets to vote on which issue. Delegates may vote differently from how their sending council might wish but are mandated to communicate the wishes of their sending council. Delegates are recallable at any time. Referendums are possible at any time via votes of most lower-level councils, however, not everything is a referendum as this is most likely a waste of time. A parpolity is meant to work in tandem with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Participatory_economy" title="Participatory economy">participatory economy</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmopolitan">Cosmopolitan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Cosmopolitan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cosmopolitan_democracy" title="Cosmopolitan democracy">Cosmopolitan democracy</a></div>
<p>Cosmopolitan democracy, also known as <i>Global democracy</i> or <i>World Federalism</i>, is a political system in which democracy is implemented on a global scale, either directly or through representatives. An important justification for this kind of system is that the decisions made in national or regional democracies often affect people outside the constituency who, by definition, cannot vote. By contrast, in a cosmopolitan democracy, the people who are affected by decisions also have a say in them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to its supporters, any attempt to solve global problems is undemocratic without some form of cosmopolitan democracy. The general principle of cosmopolitan democracy is to expand some or all of the values and norms of democracy, including the rule of law; the non-violent resolution of conflicts; and equality among citizens, beyond the limits of the state. To be fully implemented, this would require reforming existing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_organisations" title="International organisations">international organisations</a>, e.g. the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, as well as the creation of new institutions such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Parliament" title="World Parliament">World Parliament</a>, which ideally would enhance public control over, and accountability in, international politics.
</p><p>Cosmopolitan Democracy has been promoted, among others, by physicist Albert Einstein,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[215]</a></sup> writer Kurt Vonnegut, columnist <a href="/wiki/George_Monbiot" title="George Monbiot">George Monbiot</a>, and professors <a href="/wiki/David_Held" title="David Held">David Held</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daniele_Archibugi" title="Daniele Archibugi">Daniele Archibugi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[216]</a></sup> The creation of the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a> in 2003 was seen as a major step forward by many supporters of this type of cosmopolitan democracy.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Creative_democracy">Creative democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Creative democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creative_democracy" title="Creative democracy">Creative democracy</a></div>
<p>Creative Democracy is advocated by American philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Dewey" title="John Dewey">John Dewey</a>. The main idea about Creative Democracy is that democracy encourages individual capacity building and the interaction among the society. Dewey argues that democracy is a way of life in his work of "Creative Democracy: The Task Before Us"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[217]</a></sup> and an experience built on faith in human nature, faith in human beings, and faith in working with others. Democracy, in Dewey's view, is a <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">moral ideal</a> requiring actual effort and work by people; it is not an institutional concept that exists outside of ourselves. "The task of democracy", Dewey concludes, "is forever that of creation of a freer and more humane experience in which all share and to which all contribute".
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Guided_democracy">Guided democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Guided democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Guided_democracy" title="Guided democracy">Guided democracy</a></div>
<p>Guided democracy is a form of democracy that incorporates regular popular elections, but which often carefully "guides" the choices offered to the electorate in a manner that may reduce the ability of the electorate to truly determine the type of government exercised over them.  Such democracies typically have only one central authority which is often not subject to meaningful public review by any other governmental authority.  Russian-style democracy has often been referred to as a "Guided democracy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[218]</a></sup>  Russian politicians have referred to their government as having only one center of power/ authority, as opposed to most other forms of democracy which usually attempt to incorporate two or more naturally competing sources of authority within the same government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[219]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-governmental_democracy">Non-governmental democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Non-governmental democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Aside from the public sphere, similar democratic principles and mechanisms of voting and representation have been used to govern other kinds of groups. Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organisation" title="Non-governmental organisation">non-governmental organisations</a> decide policy and leadership by voting. Most <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade unions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cooperative" title="Cooperative">cooperatives</a> are governed by democratic elections. <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">Corporations</a> are ultimately governed by their <a href="/wiki/Shareholder" title="Shareholder">shareholders</a> through <a href="/wiki/Shareholder_democracy" title="Shareholder democracy">shareholder democracy</a>. Corporations may also employ systems such as <a href="/wiki/Workplace_democracy" title="Workplace democracy">workplace democracy</a> to handle internal governance. <a href="/wiki/Amitai_Etzioni" title="Amitai Etzioni">Amitai Etzioni</a> has postulated a system that fuses elements of democracy with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharia_law" title="Sharia law">sharia law</a>, termed <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Islamocracy" title="wikt:Islamocracy">Islamocracy</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[220]</a></sup> There is also a growing number of <a href="/wiki/Democratic_education" title="Democratic education">Democratic educational</a> institutions such as  <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_school" title="Sudbury school">Sudbury schools</a> that are co-governed by students and staff.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shareholder_democracy">Shareholder democracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Shareholder democracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shareholder_democracy" title="Shareholder democracy">Shareholder democracy</a></div>
<p>Shareholder democracy is a concept relating to the governance of corporations by their shareholders. In the United States, shareholders are typically granted voting rights according to the <a href="/wiki/One_share,_one_vote" title="One share, one vote">one share, one vote</a> principle. Shareholders may vote annually to elect the company's <a href="/wiki/Board_of_directors" title="Board of directors">board of directors</a>, who themselves may choose the company's <a href="/wiki/Chief_executive_officer" title="Chief executive officer">executives</a>. The shareholder democracy framework may be inaccurate for companies which have different <a href="/wiki/Share_class" title="Share class">classes of stock</a> that further alter the distribution of voting rights.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Justification">Justification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Justification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content <p>Several justifications for democracy have been postulated.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legitimacy">Legitimacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Legitimacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">Social contract theory</a> argues that the <a href="/wiki/Legitimacy_(political)" title="Legitimacy (political)">legitimacy of government</a> is based on <a href="/wiki/Consent_of_the_governed" title="Consent of the governed">consent of the governed</a>, i.e. an election, and that political decisions must reflect the <a href="/wiki/General_will" title="General will">general will</a>. Some proponents of the theory like <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a> advocate for a <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a> on this basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[221]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Better_decision-making">Better decision-making</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Better decision-making">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Condorcet%27s_jury_theorem" title="Condorcet's jury theorem">Condorcet's jury theorem</a> is logical proof that if each decision-maker has a better than chance probability of making the right decision, then having the largest number of decision-makers, i.e. a democracy, will result in the best decisions. This has also been argued by theories of <a href="/wiki/Wisdom_of_the_crowd" title="Wisdom of the crowd">the wisdom of the crowd</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_success">Economic success</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Economic success">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Why_Nations_Fail" title="Why Nations Fail">Why Nations Fail</a></i>, economists <a href="/wiki/Daron_Acemoglu" title="Daron Acemoglu">Daron Acemoglu</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_A._Robinson_(economist)" title="James A. Robinson (economist)">James A. Robinson</a> argue that democracies are more economically successful because undemocratic political systems tend to limit markets and favor <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopolies</a> at the expense of the <a href="/wiki/Creative_destruction" title="Creative destruction">creative destruction</a> which is necessary for sustained <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a>.
</p><p>A 2019 study by Acemoglu and others estimated that countries switching to democratic from authoritarian rule had on average a 20% higher GDP after 25 years than if they had remained authoritarian. The study examined 122 transitions to democracy and 71 transitions to authoritarian rule, occurring from 1960 to 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[222]</a></sup> Acemoglu said this was because democracies tended to invest more in health care and human capital, and reduce special treatment of regime allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[223]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_democracy" title="Criticism of democracy">Criticism of democracy</a></div>
<h3><span id="Arrow.27s_theorem"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrow's_theorem">Arrow's theorem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Arrow's theorem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arrow%27s_impossibility_theorem" title="Arrow's impossibility theorem">Arrow's impossibility theorem</a> suggests that democracy is logically incoherent. This is based on a certain set of criteria for democratic decision-making being inherently conflicting, i.e. these three "fairness" criteria:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<ul><li>If every voter prefers alternative X over alternative Y, then the group prefers X over Y.</li>
<li>If every voter's preference between X and Y remains unchanged, then the group's preference between X and Y will also remain unchanged (even if voters' preferences between other pairs like X and Z, Y and Z, or Z and W change).</li>
<li>There is no "dictator": no single voter possesses the power to always determine the group's preference.</li></ul>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Arrow" title="Kenneth Arrow">Kenneth Arrow</a> summarised the implications of the theorem in a non-mathematical form, stating that "no voting method is fair", "every ranked voting method is flawed", and "the only voting method that isn't flawed is a dictatorship".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, Arrow's formal premises can be considered overly strict, and with their reasonable weakening, the logical incoherence of democracy looks much less critical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tangian2020Book_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tangian2020Book-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inefficiencies">Inefficiencies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Inefficiencies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some economists have criticized the efficiency of democracy, citing the premise of the irrational voter, or a voter who makes decisions without all of the facts or necessary information in order to make a truly informed decision. Another argument is that democracy slows down processes because of the amount of input and participation needed in order to go forward with a decision. A common example often quoted to substantiate this point is the high economic development achieved by <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (a non-democratic <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party</a> ruling <a href="/wiki/Communist_state" title="Communist state">communist state</a>) as compared to <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> (a democratic <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">multi-party</a> state). According to economists, the lack of democratic participation in countries like China allows for unfettered economic growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> believed that democracy without educated masses (educated in the broader sense of being knowledgeable and responsible) would only lead to <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populism</a> being the criteria to become an elected leader and not competence. This would ultimately lead to a societal demise. This was quoted by Plato in book 10 of The Republic, in Socrates' conversation with Adimantus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[226]</a></sup> Socrates was of the opinion that the right to vote must not be an indiscriminate right (for example by birth or citizenship), but must be given only to people who thought sufficiently of their choice.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Republic_(Plato)" title="The Republic (Plato)">The Republic</a></i> presents a critical view of democracy through the narration of <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>: "Democracy, which is a charming form of government, full of variety and disorder, and dispensing a sort of equality to equals and unequaled alike."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[227]</a></sup> In his work, Plato lists <a href="/wiki/Plato%27s_five_regimes" title="Plato's five regimes">5 forms of government</a> from best to worst, and lists democracy as the second worst, behind only tyranny, which he implies to be the natural outcome of democracy, arguing that in a democracy everyone puts their own selfish interests ahead of the common good until a tyrant emerges who is strong enough to impose his interest on everyone else. Assuming that <i>the Republic</i> was intended to be a serious critique of the political thought in Athens, Plato argues that only Kallipolis, an aristocracy led by the unwilling philosopher-kings (the wisest men), is a just form of government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Xi_Jinping" title="Xi Jinping">Xi Jinping</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Chinese_Communist_Party" title="General Secretary of the Chinese Communist Party">General Secretary of the Chinese Communist Party</a>, warned <a href="/wiki/Joe_Biden" title="Joe Biden">Joe Biden</a>, <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">U.S. president</a>, via a phone call that democracy was dying. "Democracies require consensus, and it takes time, and you don't have the time," Xi Jinping added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[229]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Popular_rule_as_a_fa.C3.A7ade"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_rule_as_a_façade">Popular rule as a façade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Popular rule as a façade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 20th-century Italian thinkers <a href="/wiki/Vilfredo_Pareto" title="Vilfredo Pareto">Vilfredo Pareto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaetano_Mosca" title="Gaetano Mosca">Gaetano Mosca</a> (independently) argued that democracy was illusory, and served only to mask the reality of elite rule. Indeed, they argued that elite oligarchy is the unbendable law of human nature, due largely to the apathy and division of the masses (as opposed to the drive, initiative and unity of the elites), and that democratic institutions would do no more than shift the exercise of power from oppression to manipulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[230]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Louis_Brandeis" title="Louis Brandeis">Louis Brandeis</a> once professed, "We may have democracy, or we may have <a href="/wiki/Wealth" title="Wealth">wealth</a> concentrated in the hands of a few, but we can't have both."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[231]</a></sup>
A study led by Princeton professor Martin Gilens of 1,779 U.S. government decisions concluded that
"elites and organized groups representing business interests have substantial independent impacts on U.S. government policy, while average citizens and mass-based interest groups have little or no independent influence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[232]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mob_rule">Mob rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Mob rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> critiqued democracy in <a href="/wiki/Federalist_No._10" title="Federalist No. 10">Federalist No. 10</a>, arguing that a <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republic</a> is a preferable form of government, saying: "... democracies have ever been spectacles of turbulence and contention; have ever been found incompatible with personal security or the rights of property; and have in general been as short in their lives as they have been violent in their deaths." Madison offered that republics were superior to democracies because republics safeguarded against <a href="/wiki/Tyranny_of_the_majority" title="Tyranny of the majority">tyranny of the majority</a>, stating in <a href="/wiki/Federalist_No._10" title="Federalist No. 10">Federalist No. 10</a>: "the same advantage which a republic has over a democracy, in controlling the effects of faction, is enjoyed by a large over a small republic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Federalist10_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-Federalist10-204">[202]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> warned that "an elective despotism is not the government we fought for."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[233]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_instability">Political instability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Political instability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>More recently, democracy is criticised for not offering enough political stability. As governments are frequently elected on and off there tends to be frequent changes in the policies of democratic countries both domestically and internationally. Even if a political party maintains power, vociferous, headline-grabbing protests and harsh criticism from the popular media are often enough to force sudden, unexpected political change. Frequent policy changes with regard to business and immigration are likely to deter investment and so hinder economic growth. For this reason, many people have put forward the idea that democracy is undesirable for a developing country in which economic growth and the reduction of poverty are top priorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[234]</a></sup>
</p><p>This opportunist alliance not only has the handicap of having to cater to too many ideologically opposing factions, but it is usually short-lived since any perceived or actual imbalance in the treatment of coalition partners, or changes to leadership in the coalition partners themselves, can very easily result in the coalition partner withdrawing its support from the government.
</p><p>Biased media has been accused of causing political instability, resulting in the obstruction of democracy, rather than its promotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[235]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition">Opposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Opposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-democratic_thought" title="Anti-democratic thought">Anti-democratic thought</a></div>
<p>Democracy in modern times has almost always faced opposition from the previously existing government, and many times it has faced opposition from social elites. The implementation of a democratic government within a non-democratic state is typically brought about by <a href="/wiki/Democratic_revolution" title="Democratic revolution">democratic revolution</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratization">Democratization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Democratization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Democratization" title="Democratization">Democratization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democracy_promotion" title="Democracy promotion">Democracy promotion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hong_Kong_P1066634_(48678676473).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3888" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Hong_Kong_P1066634_%2848678676473%29.jpg/220px-Hong_Kong_P1066634_%2848678676473%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Hong_Kong_P1066634_%2848678676473%29.jpg/330px-Hong_Kong_P1066634_%2848678676473%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Hong_Kong_P1066634_%2848678676473%29.jpg/440px-Hong_Kong_P1066634_%2848678676473%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hong_Kong_P1066634_(48678676473).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Banner in Hong Kong asking for democracy, August 2019</div></div></div>
<p>Several philosophers and researchers have outlined historical and social factors seen as supporting the evolution of democracy.
</p><p>Other commentators have mentioned the influence of economic development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[236]</a></sup> In a related theory, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Inglehart" title="Ronald Inglehart">Ronald Inglehart</a> suggests that improved living-standards in modern developed countries can convince people that they can take their basic survival for granted, leading to increased emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Self-expression_values" title="Self-expression values">self-expression values</a>, which correlates closely with democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[238]</a></sup>
</p><p>Douglas M. Gibler and Andrew Owsiak in their study argued about the importance of peace and stable borders for the development of democracy. It has often been assumed that <a href="/wiki/Democratic_peace_theory" title="Democratic peace theory">democracy causes peace</a>, but this study shows that, historically, peace has almost always predated the establishment of democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[239]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Carroll_Quigley" title="Carroll Quigley">Carroll Quigley</a> concludes that the characteristics of weapons are the main predictor of democracy:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[241]</a></sup> Democracy—this scenario—tends to emerge only when the best weapons available are easy for individuals to obtain and use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quigley1983-38_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quigley1983-38-244">[242]</a></sup> By the 1800s, guns were the best personal weapons available, and in the United States of America (already nominally democratic), almost everyone could afford to buy a gun, and could learn how to use it fairly easily. Governments couldn't do any better: it became the age of mass armies of citizen soldiers with guns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quigley1983-38_244-1"><a href="#cite_note-Quigley1983-38-244">[242]</a></sup> Similarly, Periclean Greece was an age of the citizen soldier and democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other theories stressed the relevance of <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Human_capital" title="Human capital">human capital</a>—and within them of <a href="/wiki/Intelligence" title="Intelligence">cognitive ability</a> to increasing tolerance, rationality, political literacy and participation. Two effects of education and cognitive ability are distinguished:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[244]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (August 2018)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[246]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>a cognitive effect (competence to make rational choices, better information-processing)</li>
<li>an ethical effect (support of democratic values, freedom, human rights etc.), which itself depends on intelligence.</li></ul>
<p>Evidence consistent with conventional theories of why democracy emerges and is sustained has been hard to come by. Statistical analyses have challenged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modernisation_theory" title="Modernisation theory">modernisation theory</a> by demonstrating that there is no reliable evidence for the claim that democracy is more likely to emerge when countries become wealthier, more educated, or less unequal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[247]</a></sup> In fact, empirical evidence shows that economic growth and education may not lead to increased demand for democratization as modernization theory suggests: historically, most countries attained high levels of access to primary education well before transitioning to democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paglayan_179–198_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paglayan_179–198-250">[248]</a></sup> Rather than acting as a catalyst for democratization, in some situations education provision may instead be used by non-democratic regimes to indoctrinate their subjects and strengthen their power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paglayan_179–198_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-Paglayan_179–198-250">[248]</a></sup>
</p><p>The assumed link between education and economic growth is called into question when analyzing empirical evidence. Across different countries, the correlation between education attainment and math test scores is very weak (.07). A similarly weak relationship exists between per-pupil expenditures and math competency (.26). Additionally, historical evidence suggests that average human capital (measured using literacy rates) of the masses does not explain the onset of industrialization in France from 1750 to 1850 despite arguments to the contrary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[249]</a></sup> Together, these findings show that education does not always promote human capital and economic growth as is generally argued to be the case. Instead, the evidence implies that education provision often falls short of its expressed goals, or, alternatively, that political actors use education to promote goals other than economic growth and development.
</p><p>Some scholars have searched for the "deep" determinants of contemporary political institutions, be they geographical or demographic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[251]</a></sup> More inclusive <a href="/wiki/Institution" title="Institution">institutions</a> lead to democracy because as people gain more power, they are able to demand more from the elites, who in turn have to concede more things to keep their position.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This virtuous circle may end up in democracy.
</p><p>An example of this is the disease environment. Places with different mortality rates had different populations and productivity levels around the world. For example, in Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Tsetse_fly" title="Tsetse fly">tsetse fly</a>—which afflicts humans and livestock—reduced the ability of Africans to plow the land. This made Africa less settled. As a consequence, political power was less concentrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[252]</a></sup> This also affected the colonial institutions European countries established in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[253]</a></sup> Whether colonial settlers could live or not in a place made them develop different institutions which led to different economic and social paths. This also affected the distribution of power and the collective actions people could take. As a result, some African countries ended up having democracies and others <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autocracies" title="Autocracies">autocracies</a>.
</p><p>An example of geographical determinants for democracy is having access to coastal areas and rivers. This natural endowment has a positive relation with economic development thanks to the benefits of <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[254]</a></sup> Trade brought economic development, which in turn, broadened power. Rulers wanting to increase revenues had to protect property-rights to create incentives for people to invest. As more people had more power, more concessions had to be made by the ruler and in many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (August 2018)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> places this process lead to democracy. These determinants defined the structure of the society moving the balance of political power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup>
</p><p>Robert Michels asserts that although democracy can never be fully realised, democracy may be developed automatically in the act of striving for democracy:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The peasant in the fable, when on his deathbed, tells his sons that a treasure is buried in the field. After the old man's death the sons dig everywhere in order to discover the treasure. They do not find it. But their indefatigable labor improves the soil and secures for them a comparative well-being. The treasure in the fable may well symbolise democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[256]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disruption">Disruption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Disruption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some democratic governments have experienced sudden <a href="/wiki/State_collapse" title="State collapse">state collapse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Regime_change" title="Regime change">regime change</a> to an undemocratic form of government. Domestic military coups or rebellions are the most common means by which democratic governments have been overthrown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siaroff_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siaroff-259">[257]</a></sup> (See <a href="/wiki/List_of_coups_and_coup_attempts_by_country" title="List of coups and coup attempts by country">List of coups and coup attempts by country</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_civil_wars" title="List of civil wars">List of civil wars</a>.) Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Coup_of_18_Brumaire" title="Coup of 18 Brumaire">Coup of 18 Brumaire</a> that ended the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_French_Republic" title="First French Republic">First French Republic</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/28_May_1926_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="28 May 1926 coup d'état">28 May 1926 coup d'état</a> which ended the <a href="/wiki/First_Portuguese_Republic" title="First Portuguese Republic">First Portuguese Republic</a>. Some military coups are supported by foreign governments, such as the <a href="/wiki/1954_Guatemalan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1954 Guatemalan coup d'état">1954 Guatemalan coup d'état</a> and the <a href="/wiki/1953_Iranian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1953 Iranian coup d'état">1953 Iranian coup d'état</a>. Other types of a sudden end to democracy include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Invasion" title="Invasion">Invasion</a>, for example the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_Czechoslovakia" title="German occupation of Czechoslovakia">German occupation of Czechoslovakia</a>, and the fall of <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnam</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Self-coup" title="Self-coup">Self-coup</a>, in which the leader of the government extra-legally seizes all power or unlawfully extends the term in office. This can be done through:
<ul><li>Suspension of the constitution by decree, such as with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1992_Peruvian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1992 Peruvian coup d'état">1992 Peruvian coup d'état</a></li>
<li>An "electoral self-coup" using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Election_fraud" title="Election fraud">election fraud</a> to obtain re-election of a previously fairly elected official or political party. For example, in the <a href="/wiki/1999_Ukrainian_presidential_election" title="1999 Ukrainian presidential election">1999 Ukrainian presidential election</a>, <a href="/wiki/2003_Russian_legislative_election" title="2003 Russian legislative election">2003 Russian legislative election</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2004_Russian_presidential_election" title="2004 Russian presidential election">2004 Russian presidential election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siaroff_259-1"><a href="#cite_note-Siaroff-259">[257]</a></sup></li></ul></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Royal_coup&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Royal coup (page does not exist)">Royal coup</a>, in which a monarch not normally involved in government seizes all power. For example, the <a href="/wiki/6_January_Dictatorship" title="6 January Dictatorship">6 January Dictatorship</a>, begun in 1929 when King <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Yugoslavia" title="Alexander I of Yugoslavia">Alexander I of Yugoslavia</a> dismissed parliament and started ruling by decree.</li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Democratic_backsliding" title="Democratic backsliding">Democratic backsliding</a> can end democracy in a gradual manner, by increasing emphasis on <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">national security</a> and eroding <a href="/wiki/Free_and_fair_election" title="Free and fair election">free and fair elections</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedom_of_expression" title="Freedom of expression">freedom of expression</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independence_of_the_judiciary" title="Independence of the judiciary">independence of the judiciary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a>.  A famous example is the <a href="/wiki/Enabling_Act_of_1933" title="Enabling Act of 1933">Enabling Act of 1933</a>, which lawfully ended democracy in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weimar_Germany" title="Weimar Germany">Weimar Germany</a> and marked the transition to <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>.
</p><p>Temporary or long-term <a href="/wiki/Political_violence" title="Political violence">political violence</a> and government interference can prevent <a href="/wiki/Free_and_fair_election" title="Free and fair election">free and fair elections</a>, which erode the democratic nature of governments. This has happened on a local level even in well-established democracies like the United States; for example, the <a href="/wiki/Wilmington_insurrection_of_1898" title="Wilmington insurrection of 1898">Wilmington insurrection of 1898</a> and African-American <a href="/wiki/Disfranchisement_after_the_Reconstruction_era" title="Disfranchisement after the Reconstruction era">disfranchisement after the Reconstruction era</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Importance_of_mass_media">Importance of mass media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Importance of mass media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information on the role of the mass media in the democratic process: <a href="/wiki/Mediatization_(media)" title="Mediatization (media)">Mediatization (media)</a></div>
<p>The theory of democracy relies on the implicit assumption that voters are well informed about <a href="/wiki/Social_issue" title="Social issue">social issues</a>, policies, and candidates so that they can make a truly informed decision. Since the late 20'th century there has been a growing concern that voters may be poorly informed because the <a href="/wiki/News_media" title="News media">news media</a> are focusing more on entertainment and gossip and less on serious journalistic research on political issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacChesney1999_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacChesney1999-260">[258]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnett2002_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett2002-261">[259]</a></sup>
</p><p>The media professors Michael Gurevitch and <a href="/wiki/Jay_Blumler" title="Jay Blumler">Jay Blumler</a> have proposed a number of functions that the mass media are expected to fulfill in a democracy:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GurevitchBlumler1990_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-GurevitchBlumler1990-262">[260]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Surveillance of the sociopolitical environment</li>
<li>Meaningful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agenda_setting" title="Agenda setting">agenda setting</a></li>
<li>Platforms for an intelligible and illuminating advocacy</li>
<li>Dialogue across a diverse range of views</li>
<li>Mechanisms for holding officials to account for how they have exercised power</li>
<li>Incentives for citizens to learn, choose, and become involved</li>
<li>A principled resistance to the efforts of forces outside the media to subvert their independence, integrity, and ability to serve the audience</li>
<li>A sense of respect for the audience member, as potentially concerned and able to make sense of his or her political environment</li></ul>
<p>This proposal has inspired a lot of discussions over whether the news media are actually fulfilling the requirements that a well functioning democracy requires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bucy1999_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bucy1999-263">[261]</a></sup>
Commercial mass media are generally not accountable to anybody but their owners, and they have no obligation to serve a democratic function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bucy1999_263-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bucy1999-263">[261]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blumler2014_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blumler2014-264">[262]</a></sup>
They are controlled mainly by economic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Market_forces" title="Market forces">market forces</a>. Fierce economic competition may force the mass media to divert themselves from any democratic ideals and focus entirely on how to survive the competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donges2014_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donges2014-265">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esser2013_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esser2013-266">[264]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tabloid_journalism" title="Tabloid journalism">tabloidization</a> and popularization of the news media is seen in an increasing focus on human examples rather than statistics and principles.
There is more focus on politicians as personalities and less focus on political issues in the popular media. Election campaigns are covered more as <a href="/wiki/Horse_race_journalism" title="Horse race journalism">horse races</a> and less as debates about ideologies and issues. The dominating media focus on <a href="/wiki/Spin_(propaganda)" title="Spin (propaganda)">spin</a>, conflict, and competitive strategies has made voters perceive the politicians as egoists rather than idealists. This fosters mistrust and a <a href="/wiki/Cynicism_(contemporary)" title="Cynicism (contemporary)">cynical</a> attitude to politics, less <a href="/wiki/Civic_engagement" title="Civic engagement">civic engagement</a>, and less interest in voting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CappellaJamieson1997_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-CappellaJamieson1997-267">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vreese2014_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vreese2014-268">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EsserMatthes2013_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-EsserMatthes2013-269">[267]</a></sup>
The ability to find effective political solutions to social problems is hampered when problems tend to be blamed on individuals rather than on <a href="/wiki/Social_structure" title="Social structure">structural causes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vreese2014_268-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vreese2014-268">[266]</a></sup>
This person-centered focus may have far-reaching consequences not only for domestic problems but also for foreign policy when international conflicts are blamed on foreign heads of state rather than on political and economic structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baum2003_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baum2003-270">[268]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altheide2002_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-Altheide2002-271">[269]</a></sup>
A strong media focus on fear and <a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">terrorism</a> has allowed military logic to penetrate public institutions, leading to increased <a href="/wiki/Surveillance" title="Surveillance">surveillance</a> and the erosion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altheide2014_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-Altheide2014-272">[270]</a></sup>
</p><p>The responsiveness and <a href="/wiki/Accountability" title="Accountability">accountability</a> of the democratic system is compromised when lack of access to substantive, diverse, and undistorted information is handicapping the citizens' capability of evaluating the political process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blumler2014_264-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blumler2014-264">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EsserMatthes2013_269-1"><a href="#cite_note-EsserMatthes2013-269">[267]</a></sup>
The fast pace and trivialization in the competitive news media is <a href="/wiki/Dumbing_down" title="Dumbing down">dumbing down</a> the political debate. Thorough and balanced investigation of complex political issues does not fit into this format. The political communication is characterized by short time horizons, short slogans, simple explanations, and simple solutions. This is conducive to political <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populism</a> rather than serious deliberation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blumler2014_264-2"><a href="#cite_note-Blumler2014-264">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Altheide2014_272-1"><a href="#cite_note-Altheide2014-272">[270]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercial mass media are often differentiated along the political spectrum so that people can hear mainly opinions that they already agree with. Too much controversy and diverse opinions are not always profitable for the commercial news media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nielsen2016_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen2016-273">[271]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Political_polarization" title="Political polarization">Political polarization</a> is emerging when different people read different news and watch different TV channels. This polarization has been worsened by the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a> that allow people to communicate mainly with groups of like-minded people, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Echo_chamber" title="Echo chamber">echo chambers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cinelli2021_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cinelli2021-274">[272]</a></sup>
Extreme political polarization may undermine the trust in democratic institutions, leading to erosion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_speech" title="Free speech">free speech</a> and in some cases even reversion to <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocracy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCoySomer2019_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCoySomer2019-275">[273]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many media scholars have discussed non-commercial news media with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_service_broadcasting" title="Public service broadcasting">public service</a> obligations as a means to improve the democratic process by providing the kind of political contents that a free market does not
provide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cushion2012_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cushion2012-276">[274]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CushionFranklin2015_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-CushionFranklin2015-277">[275]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> has recommended public service broadcasting in order to strengthen democracy in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_countries" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a>. These broadcasting services should be accountable to an independent regulatory body that is adequately protected from interference from political and economic interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buckley2008_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buckley2008-278">[276]</a></sup>
Public service media have an obligation to provide reliable information to voters. Many countries have publicly funded radio and television stations with public service obligations, especially in Europe and Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuntherMugham_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuntherMugham-279">[277]</a></sup> while such media are weak or non-existent in other countries including the
USA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[278]</a></sup>
Several studies have shown that the stronger the dominance of commercial broadcast media over public service media, the less the amount of policy-relevant information in the media and the more focus on <a href="/wiki/Horse_race_journalism" title="Horse race journalism">horse race journalism</a>, personalities, and the pecadillos of politicians. Public service broadcasters are characterized by more policy-relevant information and more respect for <a href="/wiki/Journalism_ethics_and_standards" title="Journalism ethics and standards">journalistic norms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Impartiality" title="Impartiality">impartiality</a> than the commercial media. However, the trend of <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulation</a> has put the public service model under increased pressure from competition with commercial
media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuntherMugham_279-1"><a href="#cite_note-GuntherMugham-279">[277]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Udris2014_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-Udris2014-281">[279]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thoday2018_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thoday2018-282">[280]</a></sup>
</p><p>The emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">internet</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a> has profoundly altered the conditions for political communication. The social media have given ordinary citizens easy access to voice their opinion and share information while bypassing the <a href="/wiki/Gatekeeping_(communication)" title="Gatekeeping (communication)">filters</a> of the large news media. This is often seen as an advantage for democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schulz2014_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schulz2014-283">[281]</a></sup>
The new possibilities for communication have fundamentally changed the way <a href="/wiki/Social_movement" title="Social movement">social movements</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protest_movements" title="Protest movements">protest movements</a> operate and organize. The internet and social media have provided powerful new tools for democracy movements in developing countries and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emerging_democracies" title="Emerging democracies">emerging democracies</a>, enabling them to bypass <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censorship</a>, voice their opinions, and organize protests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhuravskaya2020_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhuravskaya2020-284">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voltmer2019_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voltmer2019-285">[283]</a></sup>
</p><p>A serious problem with the social media is that they have no truth filters. The established news media have to guard their reputation as trustworthy, while ordinary citizens may post unreliable information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhuravskaya2020_284-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zhuravskaya2020-284">[282]</a></sup> In fact, studies show that false stories are going more <a href="/wiki/Viral_phenomenon" title="Viral phenomenon">viral</a> than true stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vosoughi2018_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vosoughi2018-286">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prooijen2021_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prooijen2021-287">[285]</a></sup>
The proliferation of false stories and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conspiracy_theories" title="Conspiracy theories">conspiracy theories</a> may undermine public trust in the political system and public officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prooijen2021_287-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prooijen2021-287">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCoySomer2019_275-1"><a href="#cite_note-McCoySomer2019-275">[273]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reliable information sources are essential for the democratic process. Less democratic governments rely heavily on <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censorship</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Misinformation" title="Misinformation">misinformation</a> in order to stay in power, while independent sources of information are able to undermine their legitimacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[286]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Democracy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-112"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-112">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution</a> in 1868 altered the way each state is represented in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>. It counted all residents for apportionment including slaves, overriding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three-fifths_compromise" title="Three-fifths compromise">three-fifths compromise</a>, and reduced a state's apportionment if it wrongfully denied males over the age of 21 the right to vote; however, this was not enforced in practice. Some poor white men remained excluded at least until passage of the <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a>. For state elections, it was not until the U.S. Supreme Court ruled 6–3 in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harper_v._Virginia_Board_of_Elections" title="Harper v. Virginia Board of Elections">Harper v. Virginia Board of Elections</a></i> (1966) that all state poll taxes were unconstitutional as violating the <a href="/wiki/Equal_Protection_Clause" title="Equal Protection Clause">Equal Protection Clause</a> of the Fourteenth Amendment. This removed a burden on the poor.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Portuguese_transition_to_democracy" title="Portuguese transition to democracy">Portugal in 1974</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_democratic_transition" title="Spanish democratic transition">Spain in 1975</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argentine_transition_to_democracy" title="Argentine transition to democracy">Argentina in 1983</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bolivia" title="History of Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Uruguay" title="History of Uruguay">Uruguay in 1984</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Brazil_(1985%E2%80%93present)" title="History of Brazil (1985–present)">Brazil in 1985</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chilean_transition_to_democracy" title="Chilean transition to democracy">Chile in the early 1990s</a>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://www.bl.uk/magna-carta/articles/magna-carta-an-introduction" rel="nofollow">"Magna Carta: an introduction"</a>. The British Library<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">28 January</span> 2015</span>. <q>Magna Carta is sometimes regarded as the foundation of democracy in England. ...Revised versions of Magna Carta were issued by King Henry III (in 1216, 1217 and 1225), and the text of the 1225 version was entered onto the statute roll in 1297. ...The 1225 version of Magna Carta had been granted explicitly in return for a payment of tax by the whole kingdom, and this paved the way for the first summons of Parliament in 1265, to approve the granting of taxation.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Magna+Carta%3A+an+introduction&amp;rft.pub=The+British+Library&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.bl.uk%2Fmagna-carta%2Farticles%2Fmagna-carta-an-introduction&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADemocracy"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.bl.uk/taking-liberties/articles/putney-debates" rel="nofollow">"Putney debates"</a>. The British Library<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">22 December</span> 2016</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Putney+debates&amp;rft.pub=The+British+Library&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.bl.uk%2Ftaking-liberties%2Farticles%2Fputney-debates&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADemocracy"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-refNARoP-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-refNARoP_78-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-refNARoP_78-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://www.bl.uk/magna-carta/articles/britains-unwritten-constitution" rel="nofollow">"Britain's unwritten constitution"</a>. British Library<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">27 November</span> 2015</span>. <q>The key landmark is the Bill of Rights (1689), which established the supremacy of Parliament over the Crown.... The Bill of Rights (1689) then settled the primacy of Parliament over the monarch's prerogatives, providing for the regular meeting of Parliament, free elections to the Commons, free speech in parliamentary debates, and some basic human rights, most famously freedom from 'cruel or unusual punishment'.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Britain%27s+unwritten+constitution&amp;rft.pub=British+Library&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.bl.uk%2Fmagna-carta%2Farticles%2Fbritains-unwritten-constitution&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADemocracy"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20141024130317/http://www.ait.org.tw/infousa/zhtw/DOCS/Demopaper/dmpaper2.html" rel="nofollow">"Constitutionalism: America &amp; Beyond"</a>. Bureau of International Information Programs (IIP), U.S. Department of State. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.ait.org.tw/infousa/zhtw/DOCS/Demopaper/dmpaper2.html" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 24 October 2014<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">30 October</span> 2014</span>. <q>The earliest, and perhaps greatest, victory for liberalism was achieved in England. The rising commercial class that had supported the Tudor monarchy in the 16th century led the revolutionary battle in the 17th and succeeded in establishing the supremacy of Parliament and, eventually, of the House of Commons. What emerged as the distinctive feature of modern constitutionalism was not the insistence on the idea that the king is subject to law (although this concept is an essential attribute of all constitutionalism). This notion was already well established in the Middle Ages. What was distinctive was the establishment of effective means of political control whereby the rule of law might be enforced. Modern constitutionalism was born with the political requirement that representative government depended upon the consent of citizen subjects... However, as can be seen through provisions in the 1689 Bill of Rights, the English Revolution was fought not just to protect the rights of property (in the narrow sense) but to establish those liberties which liberals believed essential to human dignity and moral worth. The "rights of man" enumerated in the English Bill of Rights gradually were proclaimed beyond the boundaries of England, notably in the American Declaration of Independence of 1776 and in the French Declaration of the Rights of Man in 1789.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Constitutionalism%3A+America+%26+Beyond&amp;rft.pub=Bureau+of+International+Information+Programs+%28IIP%29%2C+U.S.+Department+of+State&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.ait.org.tw%2Finfousa%2Fzhtw%2FDOCS%2FDemopaper%2Fdmpaper2.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADemocracy"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFNorthWeingast1989">North, Douglass C.; Weingast, Barry R. (1989). <li id="cite_note-247"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-247">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFDearyBattyGale2008">Deary, I.J.; Batty, G.D.; Gale, C.R. <li id="cite_note-248"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-248">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Compare: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFRindermann2008">Rindermann, H (2008). "Relevance of education and intelligence for the political development of nations: Democracy, rule of law and political liberty". <i>Intelligence</i>. <b>36</b> (4): 306–22. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.intell.2007.09.003" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.intell.2007.09.003</a>. <q>Political theory has described a positive linkage between education, cognitive ability and democracy. This assumption is confirmed by positive correlations between education, cognitive ability, and positively valued political conditions (N = 183–130). [...] It is shown that in the second half of the 20th century, education and intelligence had a strong positive impact on democracy, rule of law and political liberty independent from wealth (GDP) and chosen country sample. One possible mediator of these relationships is the attainment of higher stages of moral judgment fostered by cognitive ability, which is necessary for the function of democratic rules in society. The other mediators for citizens as well as for leaders could be the increased competence and willingness to process and seek information necessary for political decisions due to greater cognitive ability. There are also weaker and less stable reverse effects of the rule of law and political freedom on cognitive ability.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Intelligence&amp;rft.atitle=Relevance+of+education+and+intelligence+for+the+political+development+of+nations%3A+Democracy%2C+rule+of+law+and+political+liberty&amp;rft.volume=36&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=306-22&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.intell.2007.09.003&amp;rft.aulast=Rindermann&amp;rft.aufirst=H&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADemocracy"></span></span>
</li>

LXVI, no. 10 (6 June 2019), pp. 52–53, 56–57.  "Democracy has no clear answer for the mindless operation of bureaucratic and technological power.  We may indeed be witnessing its extension in the form of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>.  Likewise, after decades of dire warning, the environmental problem remains fundamentally unaddressed.... Bureaucratic overreach and environmental catastrophe are precisely the kinds of slow-moving existential challenges that democracies deal with very badly.... Finally, there is the threat du jour: corporations and the technologies they promote."  (pp. 56–57.)</li>
